Death Toll From Ebola Surges in West Africa, Prompting Alarm
By RICK GLADSTONE
339 words
16 July 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
New cases and deaths from the Ebola virus outbreak in the West African countries of Guinea[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/news/international/countriesandterritories/guinea/index.html?inline=nyt-geo], Liberia[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/news/international/countriesandterritories/liberia/index.html?inline=nyt-geo] and Sierra Leone[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/news/international/countriesandterritories/sierraleone/index.html?inline=nyt-geo], already the worst ever recorded for the disease,[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/02/world/africa/ebola-outbreak-is-largest-ever.html] have surged by double-digit percentages in the past week, the World Health Organization[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/organizations/w/world_health_organization/index.html?inline=nyt-org] reported Tuesday,

with no sign of a slowdown. Alarmed Ivory Coast[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/news/international/countriesandterritories/ivorycoast/index.html?inline=nyt-geo] border authorities blocked hundreds of Ivorian refugees in Liberia from returning, news agencies reported.

In its latest update[http://www.who.int.proxy.bc.edu/csr/don/2014_07_15_ebola/en/], the W.H.O. said the number of suspect, probable and confirmed cases as of Saturday totaled 964, up about 14 percent from a week earlier. Deaths totaled 603, up about 16 percent from a week earlier. Half the deaths have been in Guinea.

“This trend indicates that a high level of transmission of the Ebola virus continues to take place in the community,” the W.H.O. said in the update. “The respective Ministries of Health are working with W.H.O. and partners to step up outbreak containment measures.”

The latest figures were reported as the W.H.O. helped finalize a coordination center in Conakry, the Guinea capital, in an attempt to slow the spread of Ebola, a hemorrhagic fever[http://health.nytimes.com/health/guides/symptoms/fever/overview.html?inline=nyt-classifier] with no known cure and a death rate that can reach 90 percent. The virus first appeared in 1976 near the Ebola River in the Democratic Republic of Congo and is believed to have been spread originally by fruit bats. Gorillas, chimpanzees, forest antelopes and porcupines can also spread the virus.

Despite the latest outbreak, the W.H.O. said it was not recommending any travel or trade restrictions in the three affected countries. Nonetheless, news agencies reported Tuesday that Ivory Coast had prevented 400 refugees who had fled to neighboring Liberia during the violent 2010-11 Ivorian political upheaval from re-entering the country. Agence France-Presse said Bruno Kone, an Ivorian government spokesman, had justified the move, quoting him as saying, “We cannot be lax in this area.”

Your Evening Briefing

Welcome to NYT Now, the new app from The New York Times.

Here’s what you need to know:

• What if the Gaza war escalates?

With militants in Gaza still launching rockets and Israeli airstrikes continuing, Israel’s decision to observe a five-hour cease-fire on Thursday doesn’t remove the possibility of a ground invasion. [http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/17/world/middleeast/israel-gaza-strip.html]

Such an operation would undoubtedly raise the casualties on both sides, and there are other risks, says our Jerusalem bureau chief, Jodi Rudoren.

Israel would most likely face more international criticism, particularly given the lopsided firepower and death tolls. (It counts one death so far, the Palestinians more than 200.)

And there is also the possibility, Ms. Rudoren says, of “a long-term occupation of Gaza with responsibility for its 1.7 million impoverished residents.”

For Palestinians, she says, the fears hark back to how, in an invasion five years ago, “Israeli troops essentially bisected the strip and blockaded Gaza City.”

“That said,” she adds, “some militants relish a ground attack as an opportunity to confront the enemy directly — and to showcase what they see as its brutality to the world.”

• What’s on?

Media deals are super hot these days, with telecom and Internet companies hunting for content partners.

Today, Time Warner said it had rejected an $80 billion takeover offer[http://dealbook.nytimes.com/2014/07/16/rupert-murdoch-said-to-have-made-offer-for-time-warner/] from Rupert Murdoch’s 21st Century Fox. That’s not the end of the story: The offer was rich enough that shareholders may insist that Time Warner revisit the bid.

What would a deal mean to the average entertainment consumer?

It would put some of the biggest content providers under one roof, says Michael de la Merced, one of our business reporters.

Movie studios like Warner Brothers and 20th Century Fox and cable networks like HBO and the Fox stable of channels would all be part of one giant company.

“That would give it potentially enormous power over the kinds of movies, television shows and other types of content that are produced,” he says, “as well as how much they ultimately cost.”

• Looking good, sort of.

Big banks had warned that their profits wouldn’t shine in the latest quarter, but so far they’ve been mostly wrong.

Bank of America’s numbers[http://dealbook.nytimes.com/2014/07/16/bank-of-america-earnings-slump-43/?ref=business] today looked bad, but if you don’t count $4 billion to settle mortgage claims, they exceeded expectations.

Likewise, JPMorgan Chase, Citigroup and Goldman Sachs all reported second-quarter earnings on the positive side this week. Forecasts for Morgan Stanley are bright for Thursday.

But that isn’t a signal that the banking business is better, says Michael Corkery, a Times finance reporter.

“This wasn’t so much a case of trading revenue coming in strong,” he says. “It is more the case that it didn’t come as low as many banks had signaled it would.”

“All the banks are still warning that it’s likely to remain at these low levels for the foreseeable future.”

• When keeping it weird becomes a challenge.

Since he first riffed off “My Sharona” with “My Bologna” in 1979, Weird Al Yankovic has been relevant — and outlasted many of the pop stars he parodied — by mastering the art of the speedy satire.

But with his latest album, “Mandatory Fun,”[http://www.weirdal.com/] Mr. Yankovic is fighting to keep up with the genre he helped create, says our culture reporter Dave Itzkoff.

Weird Al’s releasing a new video daily through Monday. His spoofs of Robin Thicke, Pharrell and Lorde are already hot.

But he has lots of competition: Search “Robin Thicke parody” on YouTube and you get 139,000 results.

“Pop stars and pop songs are so much more dispensable now,” Mr. Itzkoff says, “so he has to work a lot faster to get his parodies out while audiences still care about the original song.”

Op-Talk
How Environmentalism Can Help Stop Ebola
By JAKE FLANAGIN
968 words
17 July 2014
NYT Blogs
NYTB
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All rights reserved
A relentless Ebola virus is sweeping through the West African countries of Guinea, Liberia and Sierra Leone, killing nearly 470 people to date, according to the World Health Organization.

“The virus is thought to reside normally in fruit bats,” The New York Timeseditorial board writes[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/06/09/opinion/ebola-gets-worse-in-west-africa.html], “which then spread it to other animals and, through them, to humans.”

The bats live in forests, which are for many West Africans a source of food and, with growing demand for resources like wood, a livelihood.

For many observers, that combination is loom more deadly with each passing day.

West Africa has never experienced an Ebola outbreak on the scale of the current one, writes[http://www.washingtonpost.com/news/morning-mix/wp/2014/07/08/how-deforestation-and-human-activity-could-be-to-blame-for-the-ebola-pandemic/?tid=hp_mm] Terrence McCoy of The Washington Post, and the area is “reported to have one of the world’s highest rates of regional deforestation.”

“The Guinea Rainforest has been ravaged by deforestation and has shrunk to less than one-fifth of its original size,” Mr. McCoy says.

In Liberia, a country recovering from decades of civil war, more than half of the forests — “home to 40 endangered species, including the western chimpanzee,” report[http://www.motherjones.com/environment/2014/07/we-are-making-ebola-worse] James West and Tim McDonnell for Mother Jones — have been sold off to industrial loggers by the government of President Ellen Johnson Sirleaf.

“Logging, slash-and-burn agriculture and chopping down trees for an increased demand for fire wood are all driving deforestation in Sierra Leone,” Mr. West and Mr. McDonnell write. “Where total forest cover has now dropped to just 4 percent, according to the United Nations Environment Program, which says if deforestation continues at current levels, Sierra Leone’s forests could disappear altogether by 2018.”

This is highly problematic for Ebola-stricken nations. Colin Schultz of Smithsonianwrites[http://www.motherjones.com/environment/2014/07/we-are-making-ebola-worse], “As people cut down the forest or push further into the forest for mining and other work, it brings humans and animals into closer contact.” More frequent encounters between encroaching human populations and newly habitat-less wildlife, he adds, “means more chances for viruses to jump from host to host.”

Consumption of bush meat from the forests — most often apes and monkeys, but also the aforementioned bats, wild birds, rodents, deer and forest antelope — in some West African countries is also contributing to the rapid diffusion of the disease. A Vice News broadcast says[https://news.vice.com/video/bushmeat-in-the-time-of-ebola]: “The scientific concerns surrounding the consumption of bush meat begin in our genes. Because we share 99 percent of our DNA with monkeys, the chances of infections and incurable diseases like Ebola crossing over from a monkey to a human are exponentially higher than with any other animal.”

But monkeys are only part of the problem. “A piece of bush meat would be any animal coming from the forest,” says Joseph Fair, an epidemiologist working in Liberia, interviewed for the Vice dispatch.

Many Western observers have called for a bush-meat ban, or at least the implementation of educational programs discouraging Africans from eating it. But many non-Africans don’t recognize “the need for bush meat,” Dr. Fair says. “Imagine living in the deep forest of Central Africa and/or Asia — it’s very difficult to raise domestic animals. Yet, human beings, by their nature, need protein sources. So bush meat, in many parts of the world, is still a necessary part of the diet.”

The situation is similarly fraught when it comes to logging and other resource-harvesting industries in the region. Scientists writing for the Onderstepoort Journal of Veterinary Research, published by the South African Agricultural Research Council and the University of Pretoria, “found deforested regions where locals hunted, dug for gold and farmed were most susceptible to outbreak,” according to Mr. McCoy. “The findings landed upon some dismal conclusions: The activities locals depend on the most are also what puts them at the most risk for contracting Ebola.”

It all comes down to finding effective ways for human populations to intermingle with once-pristine forest ecologies. And this, most experts agree, is contingent on finding sustainable ways for West Africans to feed themselves and their families. “Public health and agriculture experts must work together to improve agriculture and figure out how to meet Africans’ demands for animal proteins in an environmentally sustainable way,” writes[http://thebulletin.org/how-prevent-next-ebola-outbreak7312] Laura H. Kahn for the Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists. “The Ebola virus can be contained, but doing so requires that people be convinced to change behavior that earns them money and provides them food.”

This is not a project African nations can undertake on their own. The international community has a vested interest in making sure the causes of Ebola in West Africa are identified, isolated and eliminated as quickly as possible. The Haaretz editorial board writes[http://www.haaretz.com/life/health-fitness/1.585839]: “There is no vaccine and no known cure for Ebola, which was first discovered in the Democratic Republic of Congo in 1976,” more than 3,000 miles from the most recent point of outbreak in Guinea.

“The fact that this latest and biggest outbreak was in Guinea, nowhere near Congo, demonstrates that the danger of spread is real,” Haaretz warns.

“Lack of knowledge amongst the population about Ebola, the high mobility of people in this area of the world, and the wide geographic spread of cases all combine together to make it difficult to control this epidemic,” says[http://www.doctorswithoutborders.org/article/struggling-contain-ebola-epidemic-west-africa] Dr. Michel Van Herp of Médecins Sans Frontières (Doctors Without Borders). “People in these areas of West Africa are more mobile than those M.S.F. has treated in previous Ebola outbreaks in Uganda or the Democratic Republic of the Congo.”

The solution, though longterm, is clear: to keep Ebola and similar diseases out of North American and European population centers and, most important, eliminate the disease in Africa, investments in developing the continent’s conservation efforts are of literal vital importance.

The New York Times Company

Editorial
Opinion
America’s Test at the Border
By THE EDITORIAL BOARD
664 words
21 July 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
The crisis of young migrants at the Texas border is a test of American values, one of those surprise exams that history now and then throws our way: Here are 57,000 helpless children. We are a nation of 300 million. Do we spit on them, or give them blankets and beds?

It is a test that many are flunking. In Arizona, no surprise, people are losing their minds. Hearing that migrant children were being sent to the town of Oracle, a county sheriff instigated a protest that ensnared a busload of bewildered YMCA campers. A disbarred former county attorney running for governor has an ad[http://azcapitoltimes.com/news/2014/07/17/andrew-thomas-az-governor-tv-ad-gay-liberal-judges-immigration/] showing a Mexican flag swallowing up a map of Arizona and the slogan “Before It’s Too Late.”

The fever is hot in other states, too: Graffiti denouncing “illeagles” in Maryland. A mayor whipping up a bus blockade in Murrieta, Calif. The call going out on YouTube for militias to get their weapons and boots, and man up to keep the little ones on their side of the river.

In Congress, which gave up on creating an orderly immigration system, Republicans are watching President Obama struggle to get a handle on the problem, and trying very hard not to help. Their reaction is one part panic, two parts glee. Representative Phil Gingrey of Georgia is warning the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention about migrants carrying the Ebola virus. For Representative Louie Gohmert of Texas, it’s H1N1 flu virus. Senator Ted Cruz of Texas is using the crisis to demand an end to President Obama’s program deferring deportations of young people known as Dreamers. There is no time like a crisis to blow up earlier efforts to fix the system’s failures.

As the crisis emboldens demagogues in Washington, Mr. Obama has the obligation to act the grown-up. But he can be a tepid ally where unauthorized immigrants are concerned, since he sees the issue through a calculating lens that overlooks the moral urgency to act. As Republicans issue absurd calls to send in the National Guard to seal the border, it’s worth remembering that the Obama administration already did that — years before the child-migrant crisis erupted.

This volatile situation demands courage and calm. Mr. Obama has the calm, but does he have the courage? Can he send a clear message like the one given by the governor of Maryland, Martin O’Malley, who told his fellow governors: “It is contrary to everything we stand for as a people to try to summarily send children back to death.” Mayor Stephanie Miner of Syracuse wrote to Mr. Obama offering shelter in her city. The Congressional Hispanic Caucus and Nancy Pelosi, the House minority leader, are pushing back at Republican efforts to speed the children’s deportations. Clergy members in Texas and other states are teaming up to welcome and shelter the migrants.

With Republicans in a frenzy, public support for immigrants is being tested[http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2014/07/16/immigration-poll_n_5589010.html?1405522973]. Leadership is needed.

The president, who has sought $3.7 billion from Congress for humanitarian and legal aid to migrant children, has the better argument. He has every right to defend his policies on moral as well as practical grounds, to confront the Tea Party’s fear and loathing with a call to treat traumatized children as refugees and protect them from harm.

It would be good to see Mr. Obama join other Democrats and Republicans in making the moral and legal case for compassionate action, to lead a backlash against the nativist backlash.

And while he’s at it, he can reaffirm his commitment to protecting, through executive action this year, millions of immigrants who have been here for years, who deserve the chance to legalize that Congress has refused them. He made this vow before the border crisis exploded; we could all benefit from hearing him repeat it.

Meet The New York Times’s Editorial Board »

Welcome to the Morning Briefing on NYT Now.

Here’s what you need to know for Thursday:

• Another execution goes awry.

The death of a convicted murderer in Arizona took so long[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/24/us/arizona-takes-nearly-2-hours-to-execute-inmate.html] that his lawyer filed an emergency appeal more than an hour after the lethal drugs were injected.

A state witness said he was asleep and snoring, while other witnesses[http://www.macon.com/2014/07/24/3213821/ap-reporters-account-of-arizona.html] said he was gasping and snorting during the two-hour execution Wednesday. The inmate died before the court responded.

• Pressing the case for a Mideast cease-fire.

John Kerry continues his visit to the Mideast today[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/24/world/middleeast/israel-gaza-strip-casualties.html] to negotiate an end to the bloodshed in Gaza, but the U.S. secretary of state has little to show for it so far.

Separately, the F.A.A. lifted its ban[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/25/world/middleeast/faa-lifts-ban-on-us-flights-to-and-from-israel.html] on U.S. flights to and out of Tel Aviv on Wednesday, saying Israel had taken precautions to ensure airport safety.

• Tougher sanctions on Russia weighed.

European Union officials are not expected to reach consensus quickly on their debate today on restricting Russian access to capital markets and defense and energy technology.

The stronger measures were proposed after the downing of the Malaysia Airlines flight in Ukraine last week.

More remains of the 298 crash victims are being flown today to the Netherlands from eastern Ukraine for identification.

• Back to the East Coast.

President Obama wraps up a three-day swing through the West Coast with a speech at an innovative Los Angeles technical college[http://www.scpr.org/news/2014/07/23/45511/obama-s-trek-from-ritzy-fundraisers-to-la-trade-te/] today, while Michelle Obama attends a fund-raiser in Chicago.

• Reputation disaster.

The United States War College is reviewing the 2007 master’s thesis of Senator John Walsh, Democrat of Montana, after The Times found that at least a quarter of it was appropriated[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/24/us/politics/montana-senator-john-walsh-plagiarized-thesis.html] from other authors.

• Colorado’s same-sex marriage ban overruled.

A federal judge in Denver declared Colorado’s same-sex marriage[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/subjects/s/same_sex_marriage/index.html?inline=nyt-classifier] ban unconstitutional but delayed the ruling to give the state until next month to seek an appeal.

• Ebola alarm rising.

The world’s largest outbreak[http://www.bbc.com/news/world-africa-28439941] of the devastating hemorrhagic disease is worsening in West Africa, with more than 630 dead in Sierra Leone, Guinea and Liberia. Sierra Leone said its top doctor was infected.

MARKETS

• Wall Street futures are little changed. European shares are mixed, while Asian indexes ended widely varied.

• Major airlines, automakers and drug companies report their earnings today, among them United Continental and Southwest, G.M. and Ford, Eli Lilly and Bristol-Myers Squibb.

• Facebook shares reached a record high in after-hours trading on stronger-than-expected earnings. The stock is now close to double its initial offering price of two years ago.

• The I.M.F. updates its World Economic Outlook this morning.

NOTEWORTHY

• Superman turns 75.

And comic book fans are taking note at Comic-Con International[http://www.comic-con.org/], which is expected to draw 130,000 people to San Diego starting today.

They will be looking for preview shots of the “Superman vs. Batman” movie not due out until 2016, news about the new “Star Wars” movie that is shooting now, and leaks of the Marvel movie “Avengers: Age of Ultron.”

• Future of the final frontier.

Comic-Con also has a panel today on outer space and humankind’s coming adventures there.

“NASA’s Next Giant Leap,” moderated by the actor Seth Green, includes the Apollo 11 astronaut Buzz Aldrin and the Jet Propulsion Laboratory systems engineer who achieved viral fame as “Mohawk Guy” during the Curiosity Mars rover landing in 2012.

• Weird Al is No. 1.

Mr. Yankovic’s new album, “Mandatory Fun,” is his first to top the charts, according to figures released Wednesday[http://variety.com/2014/music/news/weird-al-mandatory-fun-1201267687/]. It’s also the first comedy album to do so since Allan Sherman’s “My Son, the Nut” in 1963.

• A popular bloodsucker.

Residents of the Gulf Coast city of Clute, Tex., typically slap and spray away mosquitoes this time of year, but today, they’ll flock to a 26-foot skeeter named Willie-Man-Chew.

It’s the central attraction at the Great Texas Mosquito Festival[http://www.mosquitofestival.com/], with events including fajita cook-offs, baby crawl racing and a mosquito-calling contest.

• Famed South African raceway is auctioned off.

The Kyalami racetrack north of Johannesburg held Formula One Grand Prix races for nearly two decades until 1993.

With development in the area intense, speculation is high that the track may be bought and demolished.

BACK STORY

She won with a big-band version of “Happy Days Are Here Again,” becoming the first black woman to wear Miss America’s tiara.

But 30 years ago this week, with only about six weeks left in her reign, Vanessa Williams was forced to resign. Nude photos of her surfaced, and Penthouse was publishing them.

From that low point, Ms. Williams did not just rebound. She vaulted to a level of celebrity that surpassed the typical Miss America.

She made successful R&B albums, starred in films and Broadway shows, and appeared in dozens of television roles, including four seasons on “Ugly Betty.”

She has been nominated for Grammys, Emmys and Tonys, and has a star on the Hollywood Walk of Fame.

Rick Rojas and Andrea Kannapell contributed reporting.

Nicholas Kristof
Opinion
Why Are We Ignoring a New Ebola Outbreak?
By KEN ISAACS
582 words
24 July 2014
NYT Blogs
NYTB
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All rights reserved
Three small, impoverished nations in West Africa—Guinea, Liberia, and Sierra Leone—now demand the world’s attention as the deadly Ebola epidemic spins out of control, killing hundreds and threatening millions. Caused by a highly contagious virus, the disease starts with typical flu-like symptoms, yet most often leads to a horrific death in just a matter of days.

Since the outbreak started in early 2014, the World Health Organization reports 1048 people have contracted the disease, and over 600 have died[http://www.who.int.proxy.bc.edu/csr/don/2014_07_19_ebola/en/].  But since ministries of health in these three countries do not have the capacity to survey the disease and count the dead properly, many experts believe that the numbers are much higher[http://www.reuters.com/article/2014/07/13/health-ebola-westafrica-idUSL6N0PO0V220140713].

 To put this into context, in 32 years (1976-2008) the Ebola virus infected 2,232 people in remote village areas and killed 1,503[http://www.who.int.proxy.bc.edu/mediacentre/factsheets/fs103/en/]. In a matter of months, this current outbreak has killed more than one-third as many people.

Doctors Without Borders is the only non-governmental organization working against Ebola in Sierra Leone and Guinea. The organization I work for, Samaritan’s Purse, has collaborated with the group in Liberia, but this outbreak is too much for us to take on by ourselves. We desperately need others to join this fight.

More direct medical care and emergency supplies are urgently needed. More importantly, there is a tremendous need for public awareness campaigns. Although there is no cure for Ebola, people can survive this terrifying disease, and we know mortality rates dramatically drop with early medical intervention[http://www.nbcnews.com/#/health/health-news/inside-ebola-clinic-doctors-fight-out-control-virus-].

Our team recently treated Harrison, the first Ebola survivor in his own family, and in all of Liberia. Thankfully, he had friends who had been educated about the disease. They recognized the symptoms in him and got him prompt medical care. Without this early treatment, the odds are he would have died as his family did.

Of course, the best way to survive the disease is not to get it in the first place. Treatment after infection will not stop the crisis.  It is imperative that other organizations join us in reaching communities with accurate information on what the disease is, how to avoid getting it, and what to do if they or one of their loved ones becomes ill. Public health and awareness information is available from UNICEF and WHO, but a larger effort is needed to share it with the millions currently at risk.

Currently, there are more than 48 civil society organizations in Liberia, and there are dozens more in Guinea and Sierra Leone. We need them all to help us in the fight against this dreadful disease. Likewise, I call on the international community and the donor governments of the world, particularly in Europe and the United States, to step in and recognize the very limited capacities of the ministries of health in West Africa and to help them contain this disease.

I urge all organizations with capacity in medical, public health, social mobilization and water/sanitation to help in this fight.

A disaster has descended upon West Africa, and it deserves the full attention of the international community. The world’s deadliest and most contagious disease is on a collision course with millions in major population centers.

The situation is urgent. There is no time to wait.

Ken Isaacs is Vice President of Programs and Government Relations for the international relief organization Samaritan’s Purse[http://www.samaritanspurse.org/].

C.D.C. Says Tuberculosis Lab, Shut Amid Exposure Scare, Can Continue Its Work
By DONALD G. McNEIL Jr.
593 words
25 July 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Correction Appended

Operations can resume at a government tuberculosis laboratory in Atlanta that was shut down this month as part of a moratorium[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/12/science/cdc-closes-anthrax-and-flu-labs-after-accidents.html?module=Search&mabReward=relbias%3Aw%2C{%221%22%3A%22RI%3A7%22}] on handling dangerous microbes, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/organizations/c/centers_for_disease_control_and_prevention/index.html?inline=nyt-org] announced Thursday.

The moratorium, imposed by the agency after two accidents at other laboratories that could have exposed workers to anthrax[http://health.nytimes.com/health/guides/disease/anthrax/overview.html?inline=nyt-classifier] and the lethal H5N1 flu[http://health.nytimes.com/health/guides/disease/the-flu/overview.html?inline=nyt-classifier] virus, was lifted at the lab because it has passed safety inspections and its work is crucial to treating drug-resistant tuberculosis cases all over the world, said Thomas Skinner, a C.D.C. spokesman.

Top priority for reopening will go to labs directly involved in patient care and fighting epidemics, Mr. Skinner said. For example, he said, the labs handling the Ebola and chikungunya viruses might reopen soon because the worst epidemic of Ebola in history is underway in West Africa, and cases of chikungunya disease[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/18/us/2-in-florida-said-to-catch-fever-found-in-tropics.html?_r=0] are being diagnosed all over the United States.

The health agency also named 11 outside safety experts who will advise its director, Dr. Thomas R. Frieden, on fixing shoddy procedures[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/17/us/cdc-director-admits-to-pattern-of-unsafe-practices.html] at government laboratories.

While there have been calls for a panel with broad investigative powers, like the National Transportation Safety Board, Mr. Skinner said in an email that the panel’s purpose was to advise rather than investigate. But he added that “any group member wanting to visit a lab (even unannounced) and speak to any staff member speaking in the lab will be permitted to do so.”

None of the panel’s members have ever been employed by the C.D.C. Ten of the 11 teach at American medical schools[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/news/health/diseasesconditionsandhealthtopics/medical_schools/index.html?inline=nyt-classifier] or work at state laboratories; the exception is Heather Sheeley, who works in Britain’s public health agency.

The chairmen are Joseph Kanabrocki, assistant dean for biosafety at the University of Chicago, and Dr. Kenneth Berns of the University of Florida’s medical school.

The appointments drew mixed reviews from other experts. Michael T. Osterholm, a bioterrorism expert who is director of the Center for Infectious Disease Research and Policy at the University of Minnesota, called the panel “an outstanding group.”

Amir Attaran, an expert on public health policy at the University of Ottawa, said the panel’s mandate seemed unusually vague and broad, since the news release describing it referred not just to C.D.C. labs but to “laboratories across the United States,” including private, academic and other federal ones.

He criticized the lack of foreigners as “almost jingoistic,” since an accident with an infectious agent could lead to a global pandemic.

Mr. Skinner said the decision to appoint mostly Americans was made “from a logistical standpoint.”

Correction: July 26, 2014, Saturday

This article has been revised to reflect the following correction: An article on Friday about a decision by the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention to allow a tuberculosis laboratory to reopen quoted incorrectly from comments by Thomas Skinner, a C.D.C. spokesman. He said, “any group member wanting to visit a lab (even unannounced) and speak to any staff member working in the lab will be permitted to do so.” He did not say, “speaking in the lab.”

Ebola rages in Guinea as fear keeps help at bay; Aid workers mistrusted by villagers are meeting with threats and hostility
By ADAM NOSSITER
1188 words
26 July 2014
International New York Times
INHT
6
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Eight youths, some armed with slingshots and machetes, stood warily alongside a rutted dirt road at an opening in the high reeds, the path to the village of Kolo Bengou. The deadly Ebola virus is believed to have infected several people in the village, and the youths were blocking the path to prevent health workers from entering.

‘‘We don’t want any visitors,’’ said their leader, Faya Iroundouno, 17, president of Kolo Bengou’s youth league. ‘‘We don’t want any contact with anyone.’’ The others nodded in agreement and fiddled with their slingshots.

Singling out the international aid group Doctors Without Borders, Mr. Iroundouno continued, ‘‘Wherever those people have passed, the communities have been hit by illness.’’

Health workers here say they are now battling two enemies: the unprecedented Ebola epidemic, which has killed more than 600 people in three countries since it was first detected in March, and fear, which has produced growing hostility toward outside help.

Workers and officials, blamed by panicked populations for spreading the virus, have been threatened with knives, stones and machetes, their vehicles sometimes surrounded by hostile mobs. Log barriers across narrow dirt roads block medical teams from reaching villages where the virus is suspected. Sick and dead villagers, cut off from help, are infecting others.

‘‘This is very unusual, that we are not trusted,’’ said Marc Poncin, the emergency coordinator in Guinea for Doctors Without Borders, the main group fighting the disease here. ‘‘We’re not stopping the epidemic.’’

Efforts to monitor it are grinding to a halt because of ‘‘intimidation,’’ he said. People appeared to have more confidence in witch doctors.

Health officials say the epidemic is out of control, moving back and forth across the porous borders of Guinea and neighboring Sierra Leone and Liberia — often on the backs of the cheap motorcycles that ply the roads of this region of green hills and dense forest — infiltrating the lively open-air markets, overwhelming weak health facilities and decimating villages.

It was in this rural area, 400 miles over bad road from Guinea’s capital, Conakry, where the outbreak was first spotted, and where it has hit hardest. More than 80 percent of those infected have died in this region, and Guinea has recorded more than twice as many deaths as the other countries.

In Koundony, over one-eighth of the population, including the headman, is dead; many others have fled.

There is no known cure for the virus, which causes raging fever, vomiting, diarrhea, uncontrolled bleeding in about half the cases and up to 90 percent of the time rapid death. Merely touching an infected person, or the body of a victim, is dangerous; coming into contact with blood, vomit or feces can be deadly.

Now the fear of aid workers, principally from Doctors Without Borders and the Red Cross, is helping to spread the disease, health officials say, creating a secondary crisis.

Villagers flee at the sight of a Red Cross truck. When a Westerner passes, villagers cry out, ‘‘Ebola, Ebola!’’ and run away.

Doctors Without Borders has classified 25 villages in Guinea as ‘‘red,’’ meaning they are suspected of harboring Ebola but are inaccessible for safety reasons.

As recently as April, the epidemic appeared to be under control. But in the past two weeks, its center appeared to have shifted across the border to Sierra Leone, where most of the new dead were being recorded. The sick are being hidden and the dead buried, without any protection. This past week, the Sierra Leone Health Ministry reported that its lead doctor fighting Ebola had contracted the disease.

But in Guinea, in the village of Wabengou, residents placed a tree in the road to block outsiders. They also attacked an official delegation from Conakry, rushing its cars, banging on the vehicles and brandishing machetes, according to Doctors Without Borders.

‘‘We don’t want them in there at all,’’ said Wabengou’s chief, Marcel Dambadounou. ‘‘We don’t accept their presence at all. They are the transporters of the virus in these communities.’’

He was surrounded by grim-looking men from Wabengou, standing at a crossroads on the way to his village; none of them demurred.

‘‘We are absolutely afraid, and that’s why we are avoiding contact with everybody, the whole world,’’ he said.

Doctors Without Borders has set up an emergency treatment center in the regional capital, Guéckédou, but a nurse there said the center had diminishing appeal.

‘‘Here, if the people come in, they don’t leave alive,’’ said the nurse, Fadima Diawara.

It may not help win confidence that the medical teams wear top-to-toe suits and masks, burning much of the outfit after helping a patient.

The wariness against outside intervention has deep roots. This part of Guinea, known as the Forest Region, where more than 200 people have already died from the disease, is known for its strong belief in traditional religion. The dictator who ruled Guinea with an iron fist for decades, Ahmed Sékou Touré, was only partly successful in a 1960s campaign to stamp out these beliefs, despite mass burnings of fetishes.

Addressing villagers recently in Bawa, where a woman had just died, the regional prefect from Guéckédou, Mohammed Cinq Keita, warned: ‘‘There is no root, no leaf, no animal that can cure you. Don’t be fooled.’’

Near the border with Sierra Leone, Doctors Without Borders discovered an Ebola patient who had been privately ‘‘treated’’ in the village of Teldou and then returned to his relatives in another village, possibly infecting untold others.

‘‘Extremely, extremely concerning,’’ said Sylvie Jonckheere, the charity’s doctor on the scene. A colleague in full gear lectured the villagers of Teldou as the rain started, but was met with indifference or hostile stares; some turned their backs on him.

As the aid workers drove off, the private nurse who administered a shot to the Ebola patient defended his treatment. ‘‘I couldn’t say that he had the illness,’’ said the nurse, Eduard Leno. ‘‘His body was hot, that’s all.’’

Asked why the patient had not been sent to the clinic in Guéckédou, he said: ‘‘We are in the bush here. You can’t just send someone away. How will society view you?’’

Local officials have begun a campaign to open the closed villages, but here in tiny Koundony, fear is palpable.

On a recent day, a Red Cross truck drove up to the cemetery to deliver the body of Marie Condé, 14, wrapped in heavy plastic sheeting.

As the body was carried off the truck, a high-pitched wail pierced the country stillness. ‘‘There is no cure!’’ a woman cried. ‘‘There is no cure!’’

The gravedigger, Marie’s half-brother Famhan Condé, 26, was sweating as he heaved shovels of dirt. The grave, he said, would be the 26th he had dug since the epidemic began.

‘‘We’re all scared here,’’ he said. ‘‘There’s no solution. We can do nothing. Only God can save us.’’

Business Day
A Boom in Corporate Travel, as Well as Threats to Safety
By JOE SHARKEY
873 words
29 July 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
LOS ANGELES — BOOM times are back in corporate travel, but so, too, is the anxiety.

For corporate travel managers gathering here at the annual convention of the Global Business Travel Association[http://www.gbta.org], two topics were high on the agenda: the surge in international business travel and the complex safety and security issues that arise as companies send more employees to diverse locations around the world.

This year’s convention, which has drawn about 6,700 travel managers, suppliers and others in the industry, falls during a tumultuous time for corporate travel. The trade group said that spending on business travel would rise about 7 percent this year, to roughly $1.18 trillion — $292.3 billion of that in the United States, up 3 percent. But as the industry representatives geared up over the weekend for five days of meetings, exhibitions and after-hours parties, there is a lot of sober discussion about travel safety and risk management.

“I’ve been at this 28 years — military, U.S. government, different response companies — and this is the greatest concentration of threat that I’ve ever seen, because of the frequency and severity of these incidents, but also because companies and institutions are global now,” said John M. Rose, chief operating officer of iJet[http://www.ijet.com/], a travel risk management company.

On Sunday, executives from iJet and another travel security and emergency response company, International SOS[https://www.internationalsos.com/en/], jointly presented a four-hour training session for a group of travel managers concerned about safety policies and health issues, for both highly experienced travelers and younger, novice travelers, “who tend to believe they are invincible,” said Wendy Stachowiak, director of global travel partnerships for International SOS.

Recent events have focused attention on growing corporate responsibilities for their travelers. “With everything that has happened in the last eight days, people are looking at things differently,” Mr. Rose said on Sunday. He asked the group gathered around tables in a conference room what they were hearing right now as main travel safety concerns. The first two responses, he said, were “Malaysian airliner shot down” and “outbreak of Ebola.”

In the last two weeks, a concentration of events has forced travel managers to focus more intensely on legal and ethical responsibilities for managing travel risk: the Malaysian airliner shot down by a missile in Ukraine and two other deadly plane crashes elsewhere; the halting of some air routes in the Middle East because of fears about ground combat; and the temporary suspension of some flights to Israel. In addition, the United States evacuated its embassy in Libya; street protests reached a boil in Hong Kong; and, just as disturbing to security managers, an outbreak of the deadly Ebola virus was spreading in West Africa.

“The thing that really keeps me awake is Ebola,” Mr. Rose said. That has dire implications, added Dr. Myles Druckman, medical director for International SOS, including a threat posed by returning international travelers who have been exposed to the virus. “These things were simply not on people’s radar in the past,” Dr. Druckman said.

Similar themes were sounded on Monday at a panel discussion titled “Get Me Out of Here!” sponsored by the trade association’s risk committee. “How do you plan for these things, and how do you decide what your responsibilities in general are to your traveling population?” said Pablo Weisz, a regional security manager for International SOS. “What kind of travelers do you have? Experienced? A traveler on their first or second international trip? What kind of training do those individuals need?”

Many travelers balk at keeping their companies closely apprised of their day-to-day activities and locations. But knowing where people are in a crisis is crucial, security experts say. Mr. Weisz said: “Young travelers might arrive in Beirut and announce that arrival on Facebook, but at the same time they might question why the company wants to know exactly where they are. It’s a matter of getting them used to it: Your company does need to know where you are.”

Increasingly, travel management involves legal issues, including potential criminal penalties in some countries for not maintaining adequate so-called duty of care for traveling workers. “There’s a lot of risk associated with not meeting your duty-of-care responsibilities,” Mr. Weisz said. In a lawsuit, for example, a company travel manager might be asked in court, “What did you do to protect your traveler?” he added.

With business travel growing around the world (China is likely to surpass the United States in business travel spending by 2016, the trade group says), “today you have a different dynamic in managing business travel,” said Michael W. McCormick, the Global Business Travel Association’s executive director.

“In the old days, it was Chicago to New York, New York to Los Angeles,” Mr. McCormick said. “Now, in a sense, every company is global, and they’re sending their travelers all over the world to do business.”

Foreign Desk; SECTA
Short Staff Tries to Cope With Ebola
By DENISE GRADY
841 words
29 July 2014
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
4
English
Copyright 2014 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Early this month at a hospital in Sierra Leone, Dr. Daniel Bausch and another physician found themselves alone in a ward with 55 patients infected with the deadly Ebola virus. The nurses had walked out in a dispute over extra pay to take care of the Ebola patients, and some were also staying away for fear of contracting the disease, which has killed health workers.

Dr. Bausch and his colleague, both working for the World Health Organization, did what they could.

''Even if you wanted to be the hero, you couldn't take care of 55 people,'' he said. ''We tried to attend to the most important things we could attend to in terms of people who were the sickest. To be honest, it sounds terrible, not really the sickest, but the sickest who you think have chances of surviving. If you've seen cases of Ebola before, you can see when someone is on their last gasp and trying to treat them won't save them.''

Guinea, Liberia and Sierra Leone have been struggling since March to stop what has become the largest Ebola outbreak ever, with 1,201 cases so far, including 672 deaths. The disease is causing widespread fear and disruption in West Africa, and shows no signs of being brought under control.

Liberia has closed many of its border crossings in an effort to contain the virus. Nigeria has shut down a hospital for decontamination, after a man from Liberia died there on Friday from Ebola. He had flown in from Liberia a few days earlier, and collapsed at the airport in Lagos. So far, 59 people who had contact with him are being monitored, but that figure does not include the passengers on the flight, who will also have to be tracked down.

That an infected person who may have been obviously ill managed to board a plane has stoked fears that other travelers could carry the disease to still more countries. So health officials in the region are considering the idea of monitoring people for signs of infection before allowing them to board flights leaving the affected countries, experts from the United States Centers for Disease Control and Prevention in Atlanta said on Monday.

Ebola is spread only by direct contact with bodily fluids -- blood, vomit, saliva, urine, stool -- from someone who is ill. People who are infected but do not yet have symptoms, which can include fever, diarrhea, vomiting and bleeding, are not contagious. American health officials said that they did not expect the disease to reach the United States, but that if it did, standard infection control procedures would almost certainly stop it from spreading.

Dr. Thomas R. Frieden, director of the disease centers, said in a statement, ''We do not anticipate this will spread in the U.S. if an infected person is hospitalized here but we are taking action now by alerting health care workers in the U.S. and reminding them how to isolate and test suspected patients.''

In areas where the disease has been spreading, health workers need special training to avoid infection and must wear several layers of protective clothing -- gowns, masks, goggles, boots, gloves and waterproof aprons or coveralls. The gear is suffocatingly hot and people can tolerate it for only so long in the tropics. The masks can fog up, making a risky business of, say, inserting an intravenous line or drawing blood. Another peril can come from taking off the protective gear, which must be removed according to a strict protocol.

Reports over the weekend that two American health workers in Liberia became infected despite wearing the required gear have troubled medical experts. Doctors from the disease centers said on Monday that they did not know how the two became infected. Dr. Sheik Umar Khan, a physician from Sierra Leone who was leading its efforts to fight the outbreak, has also become infected.

Dr. Bausch, an associate professor at the Tulane School of Public Health and Tropical Medicine and the head of the Virology and Emerging Infections Department at the United States Naval Medical Research Unit No. 6 in Lima, Peru, said he had worked during the current outbreak only in Guinea and Sierra Leone, not in Liberia. But he said that conditions at a hospital in Kenema, Sierra Leone -- where he was on the ward with 55 Ebola patients -- might help explain why workers are at risk.

''You need to have a controlled environment, enough health workers to take care of patients, clean the ward, provide the ancillary services required to reinforce safe practices,'' he said. ''That was definitely not there.''

It was not uncommon, he said, to find that four or five severely ill patients had fallen out of bed and were soiled with blood, stool or vomit. Several people would be needed to clean and decontaminate them and their surroundings, but the personnel were not available. In that kind of setting, he said, the risk for health workers rises.

The Ebola outbreak
380 words
30 July 2014
International New York Times
INHT
6
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Doctors Without Borders has described the Ebola epidemic sweeping across West Africa as ‘‘out of control.’’ The Ebola virus, which is fatal in 90 percent of cases, has killed more than 670 people in West Africa and spread to 60 locations in four countries. The obstacles to bringing the virus under control are formidable, among them a shortage of medical resources and resistance from local communities terrified by a disease they do not understand.

The current outbreak began in Guinea in March. Sierra Leone is now the epicenter of the epidemic. The situation is deteriorating rapidly in Liberia. On Friday, a man died of an Ebola infection in Lagos, Nigeria.

Nurses and doctors are also falling victim to the disease. Two American aid workers have tested positive, and a doctor at Liberia’s largest hospital, the John F. Kennedy Memorial Medical Center in Monrovia, has died of the disease. The virus is amplified by a mobile population, especially across the shared borders between Guinea, Sierra Leone and Liberia. In the Nigerian case, the infected victim entered on a passenger flight from Liberia. The World Health Organization and the Nigerian government have shut down the hospital where the man died, and other passengers on the flight are being tracked down and tested.

Infected individuals must be isolated in health centers to prevent the virus from spreading to others and to give them the care they need. Bodies of victims must also be disposed of with care: The virus, present in bodily fluids, including sweat, is most infectious at the end-stage. Then there is widespread ignorance among the most vulnerable populations about what needs to be done. The result is that many people are hiding sick loved ones at home and transporting bodies for burial with no understanding of the precautions they must take.

Liberia’s president, Ellen Johnson Sirleaf, has declared a national Ebola emergency. The governments of Guinea, Sierra Leone and Nigeria must also act with equal urgency to raise public awareness, put additional trained medical personnel on the ground and trace patients’ contacts with others. The current Ebola outbreak is more than a sum of national emergencies. It is now a regional crisis, and the whole of West Africa must act to contain it.

U.S.; Politics
Washington Prepares to Host Meeting of African Leaders
By MICHAEL D. SHEAR and JULIE HIRSCHFELD DAVIS
550 words
30 July 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON — Federal officials have warned government employees here that the arrival next week of 50 African leaders — and their motorcades — will bring city traffic to a standstill during a two-day White House summit meeting about issues on the continent.

In an email to top officials at federal offices, the nation’s personnel chief urged agencies to let their workers stay at home during the event, which takes place next Tuesday and Wednesday. Traffic is also expected to be snarled on Monday and Thursday as the leaders arrive and leave.

“Agencies should consider encouraging employees who can be spared from their duties to request to adjust their work schedules,” Katherine Archuleta, the director of the Office of Personnel Management, wrote Friday in a memo.

The two-day gathering was arranged after President Obama visited Africa last year. Officials have said the summit meeting will focus on increasing trade and investment in Africa, improving security, and building on the continent’s progress toward democracy.

The White House said that all African leaders who are “in good standing” with the United States and the African Union[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/organizations/a/african_union/index.html?inline=nyt-org] are included. The president did not invite President Robert Mugabe[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/people/m/robert_mugabe/index.html?inline=nyt-per] of Zimbabwe and President Omar Hassan al-Bashir[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/people/b/omar_hassan_al_bashir/index.html?inline=nyt-per] of Sudan, among several others.

The leaders are invited to a reception on Capitol Hill on Monday evening, events at the Mandarin Oriental Hotel on Tuesday morning, a dinner at the White House on Tuesday evening and a series of sessions with Mr. Obama on Wednesday. The first lady, Michelle Obama, and Laura Bush, the former first lady, will be the hosts of the African leaders’ spouses at a daylong symposium at the Kennedy Center.

Ms. Archuleta wrote that for now the federal government would remain open Monday through Thursday next week, but she advised federal employees to monitor local news outlets for announcements about potential street closings, disruptions to public transportation or a closing of the federal government.

Some of the leaders will be arriving from countries that are experiencing an outbreak of the Ebola virus, but officials at the White House and the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention said Tuesday that the government was not taking any specific precautions. “We believe air travel continues to be safe, and the C.D.C. has stated there is no significant risk in the United States from the current Ebola outbreak,” Edward Price, a spokesman for the White House National Security Council, said in an email.

The World Health Organization has said that the more than 1,000 cases of confirmed Ebola infection[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/29/world/africa/short-staff-tries-to-cope-with-ebola.html] in the West African countries of Guinea, Liberia and Sierra Leone represent the worst-ever outbreak for the deadly disease, a hemorrhagic fever that has no known cure and a death rate that approaches 90 percent.

A spokesman for the C.D.C. said procedures are already in place to stem the spread of Ebola, and flight crews and airline personnel will generally not allow a sick passenger to board a plane.

The spokesman, Jason McDonald, said, “There are no plans or indications that we’re going to take any additional precautions with the leaders who are coming.”

Morning Views
By JAKE FLANAGIN
808 words
31 July 2014
NYT Blogs
NYTB
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All rights reserved
Federal law enforcement policies on marijuana use are out of step with scientific consensus, writes Philip M. Boffey for The New York Times[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/31/opinion/what-science-says-about-marijuana.html?ref=opinion]. “Marijuana is far less harmful to human health than most banned drugs and is less dangerous than the highly addictive but perfectly legal substances known as alcohol and tobacco.” Mr. Boffey concedes that marijuana isn’t an entirely harmless substance: “The potency of current strains may shock those who haven’t tried it for decades, particularly when ingested as food. It can produce a serious dependency, and constant use would interfere with job and school performance. It needs to be kept out of the hands of minors.” But on balance, “its downsides are not reasons to impose criminal penalties on its possession, particularly not in a society that permits nicotine use and celebrates drinking.”

Other significant viewpoints from around the world:

“Relatively tough sanctions from the United States and European Union on Russian banks and oil companies, and a string of military defeats that have pushed back his proxy forces” in Eastern Ukraine constitute “a dangerous moment” for Russian President Vladimir V. Putin, says an editorial in The Washington Post[http://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/the-west-must-prepare-for-a-wounded-putin-to-become-even-more-aggressive/2014/07/30/d80efcd8-1804-11e4-85b6-c1451e622637_story.html]. The editorial adds, “He is capable of shifts of direction — such as his sudden offer last summer to help strip his ally Syria of chemical weapons.” But Mr. Putin is “more likely to escalate than back down. Ukraine and the West must be prepared for a more forceful and overt Russian military intervention.”

The arrest of foreign correspondents in Tehran is “a brutal reminder of the distance between President Hassan Rouhani’s reforming promises and his willingness to act,” says an editorial in The Guardian[http://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2014/jul/30/guardian-view-detained-journalists-iran-hassan-rouhani]. “His silence on this arrest, which took place over a week ago on 22 July, is turning the affair into a public test of the president’s ambition to usher in the more liberal era he promised barely a year ago, and his readiness to resist the determination of the conservative judiciary and ultimately Ayatollah Khamanei to block him.” The editorial calls on President Rouhani to prove his willingness to reform by freeing the journalists. Otherwise, “it will be a damaging blow to confidence in his capacity to deliver — particularly in the U.S.”

California Governor Jerry Brown signed an executive order in April that “prohibited homeowners groups from fining members for brown lawns,” reports an editorial in The San Francisco Chronicle[http://www.sfgate.com/opinion/editorials/article/California-drought-We-all-must-conserve-5658166.php]. It didn’t stop the association of a Bay Area development from asking its resident to use more water for their lawns, which highlights a problem with the state’s approach to the water shortage: “Not everyone gets it. Hardly a day goes by without another letter to the editor arriving to complain about how the writer’s earnest conservation efforts were being overwhelmed by the obliviousness of water hogs — even by some of the same state and local governments that have been pressing for mandatory cutbacks on usage.” Everyone, the editorial says, must change their ways.

“The entire world must rally to the aid of West Africa as the region experiences the biggest outbreak of the deadly Ebola virus in history,” says an editorial in The Cleveland Plain Dealer[http://www.cleveland.com/opinion/index.ssf/2014/07/the_world_must_fight_against_t.html]. “The viral disease, which has a fatality rate of up to 90 percent, has killed more than 600 people in Guinea, Liberia and Sierra Leone.” Help should include “volunteer medical teams, equipment and local outreach workers, especially as panic grows and the region’s health professionals fall prey to the disease, damaging an already rudimentary health system.” The world must devote special attention to keeping the disease out of Lagos, Nigeria, the largest city in Africa, the editorial points out. If it spreads to that city, “it could potentially hop-scotch across the world.”

In the two months since a military coup removed Yingluck Shinawatra, the former prime minister of Thailand, from power, the newly installed National Council for Peace and Order “appears to have made progress in tackling protracted problems on many fronts,” according to an editorial in The Bangkok Post[http://m.bangkokpost.com/opinion/423340]. For example, the N.C.P.O. has cracked down on “forest encroachment by the rich to turn the once wooded land into resorts, condominiums, oil palm or rubber plantations.” But the N.C.P.O. needs to shift focus on unrest in southern provinces, which anchors Thailand’s sizable Muslim community. “Many people in this region, both Muslims and non-Muslims, are living a dangerous life, not knowing when they will fall victim to a bomb blast or gunshots perpetrated by the radical militants.” There have been 26 violent incidents since the coup, the editorial adds, and the N.C.P.O.’s actions in the south “are too little given the severity of the problem and the safety of the people down there.”

Your Evening Briefing
By RICK ROJAS
1083 words
31 July 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Here’s what you need to know from NYT Now:

• Nada. Zip. Goose egg.

Between partisan bickering and intra-Republican clashes, Congress failed [http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/01/us/politics/blow-to-house-gop-leadership-as-border-bill-falters-.html]to arrive at any kind of border bill. No emergency funds. No increased security.

It hasn’t just been a rough day on the Hill, it’s been a rough year. Could it get even worse?

“No!” says our congressional reporter, Jonathan Weisman. “It’s going to get better.”

This year has been more intense than usual, he says, “because it’s a midterm in which Senate control is truly at stake, and almost every race up for grabs is a Democrat fighting on Republican turf.”

“Next year, it’s not an election year,” he says, and “really you’re not going to see presidential politics start consuming politics till the summer.”

“And in the midterms, if the Republicans take control of the Senate, they’re going to want to show they can do something.”

• The crisis of so many crises.

People are fleeing conflicts in Syria and Ukraine, South Sudan and the Central African Republic, Afghanistan and Myanmar — and that’s just some.

They are being displaced on a scale that hasn’t been seen since World War II, says Nick Cumming-Bruce, our reporter who covers the United Nations from Geneva.

And relief agencies are finding themselves desperate.

“It has put the entire United Nations humanitarian response mechanism under extraordinary strain,” Mr. Cumming-Bruce says.

In some places the lack of resources is so severe that, he notes, “the daily ration of food given to refugees is being sharply reduced.”

“There isn’t enough money in the pot to deliver what had once been the requisite amount.”

The only recourse is to go to donor countries, which claim to be tapped out.

“They ask for more,” Mr. Cumming-Bruce says, “but many programs remain critically underfunded.”

• Pitched battle against a virus.

The governments of several West African nations are desperate to stop an Ebola epidemic that started in March.

They’ve announced a range of emergency measures[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/01/world/africa/sierra-leone-declares-health-emergency-over-ebola.html], from closing schools to using security forces to enforce quarantines, after a surge of deaths in the last week brought the toll to 729.

It’s an uphill battle, says Adam Nossiter, our West Africa bureau chief, who has been reporting on the epidemic from Guinea and Nigeria.

There is no cure, and the majority of those infected die of raging fevers, diarrhea and internal and external bleeding.

“These countries — their leaders — are now quite fearful, reflecting the fears of the population,” Mr. Nossiter says.

“But their health systems are too weak to stop the outbreak,” he warns. “They need lots of outside help.”

• Argentina as precedent.

We’ve been hearing for years about sovereign default, thanks to the global economic downturn, and the efforts of debt-racked countries like Greece to persuade creditors to accept less than promised.

The case of Argentina, though, is a new wrinkle. It has ended up in its second default[http://www.nytimes.com/video/multimedia/100000003031166/argentina-default-clouds-market-horizon.html] in 12 years, squeezed by a hedge fund backed up by an American court.

“This landmark legal case may have made it harder for countries to reach deals that lessen their debt,” says Peter Eavis, a Times business reporter who is covering the default.

“The case does that, according to some experts, because it has strengthened the hand of creditors, like the hedge funds in this case.”

• Showdown in New York.

“There’s a sense that Cuomo is now being bedeviled and upstaged by a very Cuomo-like figure.”

That’s Michael Barbaro, one of our political reporters, on the examination by the U.S. attorney in Manhattan[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/31/nyregion/us-attorney-warns-cuomo-on-ethics-case-.html] of how Gov. Andrew M. Cuomo shut down his own anticorruption commission.

The situation, Mr. Barbaro says, pits two “big, swaggering, only-in-New York personalities” against each other.

Preet Bharara, the headline-grabbing U.S. attorney, has turned to some of the same tactics used by Mr. Cuomo when he was the state’s attorney general.

MARKETS

• Wall Street stocks closed down. The Dow dropped 317 points, closing at 16,563.3, and the S.&P. 500 lost 2 percent, closing at 1,930.7.

TONIGHT

• The (fictional) Israeli-Palestinian conflict.

Maggie Gyllenhaal plays a British-Israeli heiress in “The Honorable Woman[http://www.sundance.tv/series/the-honorable-woman],” a new series whose plot centers on the death of a Palestinian businessman connected to her family’s business empire.

Our TV critic Alessandra Stanleycalls the timing “disturbing,” [http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/31/arts/television/the-honorable-woman-starring-maggie-gyllenhaal.html]but says the show is “an astute, sensitive and at times delicate psychological drama that is evenhanded” (10 p.m. Eastern, Sundance).

• The reality of fantasy.

A new series, “The Quest[http://abc.go.com/shows/the-quest],” drops 12 contestants into a medieval world of dragons and ogres, where they are declared defenders of a noble cause.

They compete to save the kingdom, and the last survivor earns the title of the One True Hero (8 p.m. Eastern, ABC).

• If you’re staying up …

On “The Daily Show”: Aubrey Plaza, who stars in the coming zombie comedy “Life After Beth[http://a24films.com/films/life-after-beth/]” (11 p.m. Eastern, Comedy Central).

“The Colbert Report”: Campbell Brown, journalist and founder of the Partnership for Educational Justice[http://www.edjustice.org/], which is suing New York State to end teacher tenure (11:31 p.m. Eastern, Comedy Central).

“The Tonight Show”: Julia Roberts; Andy Cohen, host of Bravo’s “Watch What Happens: Live[http://www.bravotv.com/watch-what-happens-live]”; the actor and comedian Ron Funches[http://ronfunches.com/] (11:35 p.m. Eastern, NBC).

“Jimmy Kimmel Live”: Kerry Washington; a performance by Tom Petty & the Heartbreakers, who have a new album[https://itunes.apple.com/us/album/hypnotic-eye/id885180084] (11:35 p.m. Eastern, ABC).

The Lollapalooza Music Festival[http://www.lollapalooza.com/] — started by the alt-rock vanguard Perry Farrell in 1991 — takes over Grant Park in downtown Chicago.

The headliners are Eminem (Friday), Outkast (Saturday) and Kings of Leon (Sunday). But there are many, many more acts all weekend long.

Even the heavily secretive, fast-rising new British electro-soul collective Jungle[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/13/arts/music/identities-revealed-jungle-is-releasing-a-new-album.html] is making an appearance.

news
A message for leaders of Africa; Listening Post
By MARK LANDLER
736 words
1 August 2014
International New York Times
INHT
2
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
When Barack Obama made his first trip as president to Africa in July 2009, he memorably told lawmakers in Ghana that ‘‘Africa doesn’t need strongmen; it needs strong institutions.’’ Next week, several of those strongmen will convene in Washington as part of a landmark gathering of nearly 50 African leaders.

The question, Africa experts are asking, is what Mr. Obama will tell them. ‘‘Are we going to have a strong message for these strongmen?’’ said Vera Songwe, the World Bank’s country director for Senegal, Cape Verde, Gambia, Guinea-Bissau and Mauritania.

The answer, White House officials say, is a resounding yes: The meeting, the largest of its kind on American soil, will send ‘‘a very tangible signal of elevating Africa within American foreign policy,’’ said Benjamin J. Rhodes, the deputy national security adviser. ‘‘This is a way to put Africa on the same footing as other areas of the world.’’ Still, given the attendance of iron-fisted leaders from Angola and Equatorial Guinea — to say nothing of American partners with checkered records, like the leaders of Rwanda and Uganda — it is not exactly the message that human rights activists would like.

The centerpiece of the three-day summit meeting will be a high-octane session to stimulate trade and investment. The leaders will be put together with dozens of deal-hungry American chief executives. Former President Bill Clinton will speak, as will the former mayor of New York City, Michael R. Bloomberg, whose foundation is a co-sponsor.

The White House will push initiatives like Power Africa, a privately funded plan to bring electricity to 20 million households across sub-Saharan Africa. Companies have pledged $9 billion, and the White House is likely to announce more backing at the summit meeting.

Though American officials are loath to admit it, the model for this approach is China, which has been spending lavishly in Africa, mainly to lock up mineral resources for its booming economy.

With such a heavy emphasis on commerce, however, some activists say the meeting will miss a chance to press African leaders on civil liberties and human rights abuses.

‘‘No African leader is going to say no to an invitation from Obama to come to Washington,’’ said Anneke Van Woudenberg, a senior researcher for Human Rights Watch, who specializes in the Democratic Republic of Congo, although the leaders of Liberia and Sierra Leone elected to stay home to deal with the growing Ebola threat. ‘‘So in that sense, it’s a real missed opportunity.’’

The White House notes that it has made governance one of the three main themes of the meeting, along with trade and investment, and peace and security.

Human rights and the rule of law, officials said, will be an inevitable subtext in all the discussions. ‘‘As longstanding friends, it’s important that we speak to each other candidly,’’ the national security adviser, Susan E. Rice, said in a speech Wednesday at the United States Institute for Peace.

‘‘For all that Africa has achieved, progress has not come fast enough or spread far enough,’’ she said. ‘‘Discrimination and habits of corruption still undermine many countries’ ability to govern effectively.’’ Other experts said the administration was right not to call too much attention to the sins of its guests. The more subtle message, they said, will be to remind Africans that the best American companies want to invest only in places with a reliable rule of law.

‘‘It would be a huge mistake to lecture on the democracy and human rights agenda,’’ said Jennifer G. Cooke, director of the Africa program at the Center for Strategic and International Studies.

Yet human rights considerations clearly played a role in assembling the guest list. The United States did not invite the leaders of Sudan, Eritrea, the Central African Republic or Zimbabwe, all of whom are on bad terms with the African Union. Egypt was invited belatedly after its election restored it to good standing with the African Union.

Egypt’s new president, Abdel-Fattah el-Sisi, declined the invitation and will send his prime minister instead — in part because none of the leaders will get a one-on-one meeting with Mr. Obama. Administration officials said that was simply a matter of logistics; the leaders will have to make do with a handshake and a few words of greeting.

The Party’s Over When the Flesh-Eating Virus Arrives
By BEN KENIGSBERG
307 words
1 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
CABIN FEVER

Patient Zero

Opens on Friday

Directed by Kaare Andrews

1 hour 35 minutes; not rated

Tickets to “Cabin Fever: Patient Zero”[https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=5iJkmT3ykes&feature=youtu.be] should come with saltines and ginger ale, though the nausea factor is less extreme — and the filmmaking less auspicious — than that of “Cabin Fever,”[http://www.nytimes.com/movie/review?res=9E06E1D6133BF931A2575AC0A9659C8B63] which opened in 2003, and “Cabin Fever 2: Spring Fever,” which bypassed a theatrical release.

Once again, the villain is a flesh-eating virus. At a remote island disease research center controlled by the Dominican Republic, Dr. Edwards (Currie Graham) hopes to extract a cure from Porter (Sean Astin[http://movies.nytimes.com/person/2649/Sean-Astin?inline=nyt-per]), an imprisoned, asymptomatic patient. This supposedly uninhabited island is where Marcus (Mitch Ryan) and his friends have the misfortune to go ashore for a bachelor party.

Less methodical and witty than its predecessors, “Patient Zero” often turns its infected characters into mindless, lurching zombies. Some shots are so dark or shaky that it’s difficult to tell what’s going on. The medical staff (apart from a voice of reason, played by Solly Duran) shows little regard for patient safety or common sense.

Like George A. Romero’s similarly claustrophobic “Day of the Dead,”[http://www.nytimes.com/1985/07/03/movies/film-day-of-the-dead.html] “Patient Zero” has allegorical overtones. Porter speaks of rendition and rights. Even taken literally, the movie could hardly ask for better timing, in light of the recent accidents[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/12/science/cdc-closes-anthrax-and-flu-labs-after-accidents.html] at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention and an Ebola outbreak[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/01/world/africa/sierra-leone-declares-health-emergency-over-ebola.html?hp&action=click&pgtype=Homepage&version=HpSum&module=first-column-region®ion=top-news&WT.nav=top-news].

Better actors would have helped. Playing to the cheap seats, the film reaches its nadir during a brawl in which a decaying buxom lab assistant (Lydia Hearst) and Marcus’s brother’s ailing girlfriend (Jillian Murray) literally rip each other apart. Up next: Russ Meyer’s “Cabin Fever.”[http://movies.nytimes.com/movie/281703/Cabin-Fever-Movie-/overview]
