Congress may be on break, but four states have congressional primaries: Kansas, Michigan, Washington and Missouri.

The most prominent name facing a challenge[http://wapo.st/1p4sq9q] is Senator Pat Roberts in Kansas.

But his campaign doesn’t seem very worried about a long-shot Tea Party primary challenger, Milton Wolf, a radiologist who is a distant cousin of President Obama’s.

Health
Symptoms of 3 New York Patients Raise Concerns About Ebola
By DENISE GRADY and MARC SANTORA
771 words
5 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Heightened concern about the Ebola virus has led to alarms being raised at three hospitals in New York. But so far, no Ebola cases have turned up.

The latest episode involved a man who had recently been to West Africa, and who went to the emergency room at Mount Sinai Hospital[http://www.mountsinai.org/] in Manhattan late Sunday with a high fever and gastrointestinal problems, the hospital reported on Monday. He is being kept in isolation at the hospital while tests are being done for Ebola, a deadly disease, but also for other illnesses that could cause his symptoms.

But the city health department issued a statement on Monday saying that after consulting with Mount Sinai and the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention in Atlanta, “the health department has concluded that the patient is unlikely to have Ebola. Specimens are being tested for common causes of illness and to definitively exclude Ebola. Testing results will be made available by C.D.C. as soon as they are available.”

At NYU Langone Medical Center[http://www.med.nyu.edu/] last week, a patient who went to the emergency room with a fever and who mentioned a recent visit to West Africa was given a mask and moved to a secluded area, said Dr. Michael Phillips, the hospital’s director of Infection Prevention and Control. But further questioning revealed that the patient had not visited any of the affected countries, “so we stopped right there,” Dr. Phillips said.

At Bellevue Hospital [http://www.med.nyu.edu/patients-visitors/our-hospitals/bellevue-hospital-center]Center[http://www.med.nyu.edu/patients-visitors/our-hospitals/bellevue-hospital-center] last week, a patient was placed in isolation, but it quickly became clear that he did not have Ebola.

An Ebola outbreak centered mainly in three West African countries — Sierra Leone, Guinea and Liberia — has infected more than 1,300 people and killed more than 700 of them. American health officials have advised against nonessential travel to the three countries, and have urged doctors to be on high alert for people who return from the region with symptoms like fever, diarrhea and vomiting.

A Mount Sinai spokeswoman, Dorie Klissas, said that to protect the patient’s privacy the hospital was declining to make public his occupation, which country he had been in, whether he had been exposed to a patient with Ebola there, or whether he had close contacts like family members, friends or co-workers who were also at risk. Officials said they expected the results of the tests for Ebola within 24 to 48 hours.

In a statement to employees, hospital officials said that Ebola was spread only by direct contact with bodily fluids, and that infection control measures were being employed to protect patients and staff members.

In the Bellevue Hospital Center case, Dr. Ross Wilson, the chief medical officer at the New York City Health and Hospitals Corporation, said that the man had symptoms also found in Ebola patients. He had arrived at Kennedy Airport from West Africa and was being detained by security personnel at the airport for an unrelated matter when he fell ill.

“He developed a headache and fever,” Dr. Wilson said. He was transported to Bellevue, but the people who brought him there did not suspect Ebola.

“We immediately put the dots together,” Dr. Wilson said.

Following the guidance of the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, every patient entering one of the city’s 11 hospitals who has fever, headache and other symptoms associated with Ebola (as well as countless other ailments), is asked two new questions.

“Have you traveled to or from West African countries in the last 10 days? Have you been in contact with an Ebola patient or with anyone who has been in contact with an Ebola patient?”

When someone is suspected of having the infection, there are three key things that doctors rely on to make the diagnosis, Dr. Wilson said.

First, the history of travel and contacts is critical. Second is whether the symptoms match those of Ebola.

Finally, there are blood tests. There are only two facilities in the country that can perform these kinds of blood tests, including at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention.

The blood tests are exceedingly complicated, he said, and there are different tests depending on the degree of sickness of the patient. Generally, he said, a 100 percent confirmation can take several days.

A patient went to Mount Sinai Hospital in Manhattan on Sunday with symptoms consistent with Ebola. | Tina Fineberg for The New York Times

news
Steps to rein in Ebola are tested by custom and gaps in security
By ADAM NOSSITER
1009 words
5 August 2014
International New York Times
INHT
1
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
The house was supposed to be under strict quarantine. Meals were to be delivered by the health department. Nobody was to go in or out.

But the enforcement, part of the government’s tough new measures to contain Ebola, was light. Two police officers hung about chatting, out of sight of the house. Visitors came and went, including family members and well-wishers who were not supposed to be there. On the front porch sat the suspected Ebola victim’s father and mother, in whose arms the young woman had died.

They had handled her body, a serious medical risk. But days after her daughter’s death, Anna Conté left to fill her plastic teapot from the communal water tap used by dozens of others. Her government minders seemed not to notice.

Alarmed by the world’s worst outbreak of Ebola, West Africa leaders have declared extraordinary measures to fight the spread of the virus in recent days, including closing schools and authorizing house-to-house searches for infected people. Here in Sierra Leone, the nation hit hardest by the disease, the government has decreed a state of emergency — telling families to stay at home on Monday for ‘‘reflection, education and prayers’’ — and ordering strict measures, including the quarantine edict.

‘‘The very essence of our nation is at stake,’’ President Ernest Koroma said in a televised speech.

But that tough stance may be undermined by a looseness in follow-through — sharply in evidence in the impoverished Kissi neighborhood in this dense seaside capital —that is deeply worrying to doctors and health care workers.

‘‘Ebola doesn’t permit a halfway engagement,’’ said Walter Lorenzi, the Doctors Without Borders mission chief here. His group, the only international organization effectively battling the disease on the front lines, has declared the Ebola epidemic out of control in Sierra Leone and its West African neighbors.

‘‘It’s not astonishing that it is spreading,’’ Mr. Lorenzi added.

As of July 30, 826 people had died in the region, easily the highest Ebola death toll ever. In only two days, from July 28 to July 30, 58 more deaths were reported.

Sierra Leone has recorded the world’s highest number of cases, 574, according to the World Health Organization. On Monday, the news media in Nigeria reported a second case there, announced by the authorities, after the death of a Liberian-American in Lagos last week. To stem the dangers of infection by handling the bodies of Ebola victims, Liberia has ordered the corpses cremated.

The streets of Freetown were empty and eerily quiet Monday for the government’s ‘‘stay at home day.’’

There is no cure. The only hope of stopping the disease is strict isolation of victims and monitoring of those in their immediate orbit. But that was not happening in the household of the Koromas, who are not related to the president. Five of its members were supposed to be under quarantine. But there were at least eight people on Sunday afternoon on the tiny veranda overlooking a muddy slope leading to Cline Bay.

‘‘The neighbors were there,’’ said a nutritionist from the government Health Ministry, who had come to deliver the family’s meals — placing food on the veranda, along with disposable plates and plastic utensils. ‘‘That’s the concern. They are not isolated. The family, the neighbors, they come to sympathize; they don’t understand.’’

She declined to give her name, saying she was not authorized to speak with reporters.

At least three other houses in Freetown are supposed to be under the quarantine measure, the official said. So far, the capital has been spared — one of the Koroma family’s children, Saudatu, is the only suspected death in the city — but the disease rages in Sierra Leone’s east: 252 deaths, the second highest total after far more populous Guinea, where the epidemic started.

Since arriving in this country, the center of the disease has now shifted to this small impoverished land on the West African coast. In the city of Kenema, where the government has attempted to set up a treatment center, some 15 to 20 health care workers, including the country’s lead doctor, have died.

But the problem is not just with the government’s enforcement of its own measures. The very existence of Ebola continues to be challenged by the population it is afflicting.

‘‘It was not Ebola that killed her,’’ said Sule Koroma, of his 32-year-old sister, Saudatu, who was angry about being confined to the house. ‘‘She did not die from Ebola. It was witch-gun business,’’ he said. ‘‘They fire’’ on his sister, said Sule, 24. ‘‘Put a curse on her, so this embarrassment come up.’’

The other family members all agreed: It was not Ebola that killed Saudatu, they said. Just up the street, a government warning poster on Ebola had been mostly ripped from the wall; a few blocks away a Health Ministry truck with loudspeakers warned the residents of its dangers. But nobody on the bustling street Sunday paid any attention.

That disbelief informed the Koroma family’s handling of Saudatu’s final days, which achieved notoriety in the local news media here. She was being treated in a Freetown hospital when the family, despairing at her worsening condition, came and took her out. Her removal sparked alarm in the city.

‘‘We told the doctor we are going to go,’’ said Sule, seated on the veranda in Kissi Sunday. ‘‘He said go and do what you want to do,’’ Sule said. The doctors had initially told them Saudatu was suffering from malaria or typhoid fever.

‘‘So we removed our sister, so we could go on to the native side,’’ Sule said. ‘‘Because we have tried the English way. And it did not work.’’

Saudatu’s condition worsened. The family put her in an ambulance, back to the hospital. ‘‘On the way going then, the lady died,’’ Sule said..

Your Tuesday Briefing

Welcome to the Morning Briefing on NYT Now.

Here’s what you need to know for Tuesday:

• A Mideast truce?

Israel and Hamas appear to be respecting the latest cease-fire[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/world/middleeast/israel-gaza.html], which began at 8 a.m. local time today.

During what is supposed to be a 72-hour truce, Egypt will host negotiations aimed at working out a long-term deal.

President Obama signed a bill giving an additional $225 million[http://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2014/08/04/us/politics/ap-us-obama-iron-dome.html] in American taxpayer dollars for Israel’s Iron Dome missile defense system.

• The Ebola outbreak.

The World Bank says it is giving $200 million[http://www.bbc.com/news/world-africa-28652083] in emergency assistance to Liberia, Sierra Leone, Guinea and the World Health Organization.

An infected American nurse is arriving in Atlanta[http://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2014/08/05/us/ap-us-americans-ebola.html] today, joining a doctor who is being treated[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/03/us/kent-brantley-nancy-writebol-ebola-treatment-atlanta.html] at an isolation unit at Emory University Hospital.

• The president’s day.

Ebola will also be a topic for discussion for President Obama on the second day of the U.S.-Africa Leaders Summit meeting in Washington.

He’ll speak at the U.S.-Africa Business Forum this afternoon before hosting nearly 50 African heads of state for dinner at the White House.

• Summer polling.

The Tea Party has a 43-year-old radiologist, a distant cousin of President Obama[http://www.npr.org/blogs/itsallpolitics/2013/10/09/230978346/obama-has-a-tea-party-cousin-and-hes-running-for-senate], on the ballot today against a three-term senator in a Republican primary in Kansas.

Primary voters also go to the polls[http://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2014/08/05/us/politics/ap-us-primary-election.html] in Michigan (races for governor, United States Senate, and 14 members of the House of Representatives); Washington State (10 House seats); and Missouri (eight House contests).

• Formula One trial close to settlement.

German prosecutors today accepted a 100 million euro offer[http://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-28656050] by Bernie Ecclestone, the head of the Formula One car-racing empire, to end his trial on bribery charges.

• Trial in front-porch shooting.

A homeowner who fatally shot an unarmed woman in Detroit is expected to be back on the stand today.

On Monday[http://www.freep.com/article/20140804/NEWS02/308040114/Theodore-Wafer-Renisha-McBride], he told the court: “I didn’t want to be a victim in my own house.”

MARKETS

• Wall Street futures are edging ahead. European indexes are gaining, and Asian markets ended mixed.

• Shares in the Chinese owner of Smithfield Foods, WH Group, jumped 6.5 percent[http://dealbook.nytimes.com/2014/08/04/shares-of-chinese-pork-producer-soar-in-trading-debut/] in their debut in Hong Kong today. Last year’s $4.7 billion purchase of the biggest U.S. pork producer was the biggest ever Chinese buyout of an American company.

• The appeal of old-fashioned print newspapers will be tested today when Tribune Publishing, owner of dailies like The Chicago Tribune and The Los Angeles Times, begins trading on the New York Stock Exchange.

• Mercedes-Benz offices in Shanghai were raided by the Chinese antitrust authorities, the latest Western company to be targeted. Qualcomm and Microsoft were named in investigations last week.

NOTEWORTHY

• Te da, gi and po.

Those are some of the 5,000 new words[http://bigstory.ap.org.proxy.bc.edu/article/scrabblers-rejoice-5000-new-words-are-way] being added to “The Official Scrabble Players Dictionary,” Merriam-Webster announced.

• “Having a beer with Nixon.”

From today through Saturday, portions of tapes in which President Richard M. Nixon describes his fall from power will be released.

“This is as close to what anybody is going to experience sitting down and having a beer with Nixon, sitting down with him in his living room,” a former aide said[http://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2014/08/05/us/ap-us-nixon-resignation-tapes.html].

• Those other college rankings.

Princeton Review announced its list of party schools[http://abcnews.go.com/US/wireStory/syracuse-university-ranks-party-school-24834468], and Syracuse University is top of the class, followed by the University of Iowa (last year’s winner), the University of California at Santa Barbara, West Virginia University and the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign.

• Showing the money.

The World’s Fair of Money, among the largest gatherings of coin collectors[http://www.worldsfairofmoney.com], begins today in Chicago.

The numismatists gather a day after five ancient Greek coins[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/nyregion/ancient-coins-returned-to-greece-ending-us-ordeal.html] worth around $200,000 that had nearly been sold at auction at the Waldorf-Astoria were returned to the Greek government.

• Greenlighted for history.

At the intersection of 105th Street and Euclid Avenue in Cleveland, the first electric traffic light was installed 100 years ago today.

The signal had red and green lights and a buzzer to alert drivers to a light change, and was operated manually from a nearby control booth.

BACK STORY

In 1976, a 27-year-old doctor in Belgium examined a blood sample from the former Belgian Congo, where a mysterious disease had broken out.

“We saw a gigantic wormlike structure — gigantic by viral standards,” Peter Piot told the BBC[http://www.bbc.com/news/magazine-28262541]. It was his first look at what would become known as Ebola.

Dr. Piot then led a medical team to a village in what is now the Democratic Republic of Congo.

Tracing every case, they identified a connector: a small hospital run by a Belgian Catholic priest and nuns. Transmission was linked to the reuse of needles at a clinic for new mothers, as well as contact with the bodies of the dead.

Quarantines halted the outbreak, but not before several hundred people had died. Debates over the source of the virus continue, with bats now seen as the likeliest reservoir.

The Belgian medical team had to name the virus. They didn’t want to stigmatize the village, Yambuku, so they took out a map, Dr. Piot said.

The nearest river they could see was the Ebola.

Victoria Shannon, Rick Rojas and Andrea Kannapell contributed reporting.

Your Evening Briefing
By RICK ROJAS
966 words

Here’s what you need to know from NYT Now:

• Friend or foe?

The major general who was fatally shot[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/06/world/asia/afghanistan-attack.html] by an Afghan soldier may be the highest-ranking member of the United States military to be killed in conflict in decades.

But the way he died has been anything but rare in Afghanistan, says Matthew Rosenberg, our correspondent in Afghanistan.

“It’s a persistent fact of life for American and allied NATO service members,” he says. “One day, an Afghan soldier might turn around and shoot them.”

Insider attacks have diminished from a 2012 peak, but it’s difficult to keep increasing precautions without further alienating the Afghan soldiers whom coalition forces are responsible to train.

The Afghans, Mr. Rosenberg says, “see that as an insulting thing, that they’re all in body armor, hanging out with them in casual situations.”

• Help for a few.

An experimental drug[http://www.cnn.com/2014/08/04/health/experimental-ebola-serum/index.html], never tested in humans, appears to be helping an American doctor and nurse infected with Ebola in Liberia, who are now being treated in Atlanta.

But that wasn’t an option for the almost 900 people who have died in the epidemic spreading in West Africa. Hundreds more are sick.

“One could see how Africans would look at the fact that foreigners got access to the drugs, and it would look like preferential treatment,” says Lydia Polgreen, a deputy foreign editor who was previously posted in Africa.

That would fit in with the African experience of subjugation from the slave trade, colonialism and apartheid.

But receiving an untested Ebola treatment could stir memories of unethical medical experiments[http://www.nytimes.com/2007/07/31/opinion/31washington.html] by whites or Westerners on “less-than-willing or not-well-informed subjects,” she tells us.

“Some might wonder if they were being used as guinea pigs.”

• Money walks.

The German news media was quick to seize on the disconnect after the billionaire head of Formula One settled his bribery trial in Munich today by offering to pay $100 million[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/06/world/europe/formula-one-chief-settles-bribery-case-for-100-million.html].

John F. Burns, who covers Formula One auto racing for The Times, says one leading newspaper called it an “obscene deal.” Another said it was a “corruption” of the German Constitution.

The charge stemmed from a $44 million payment that Bernie Ecclestone, who runs the racing empire, made in 2006 to a German banker. The banker was jailed. For his part, Mr. Ecclestone said he had been blackmailed.

Many Germans, however, see “a kind of justice available only to people as wealthy as Mr. Ecclestone,” says Mr. Burns.

MARKETS

• Wall Street stocks closed down. The S.&P. 500 lost 1 percent.

TONIGHT

• Taking to the streets.

It’s National Night Out[http://natw.org/about/], with events held in hundreds of cities around the country to bring residents out to meet the police and learn about anticrime efforts.

The night out falls every year on the first Tuesday in August, except in Texas, which has its events on the first Tuesday in October.

• A mini-binge opportunity.

Six 22-minute episodes of “Sequestered[http://www.crackle.com/c/sequestered?cmpid=4268&gclid=CO_ErKGD_L8CFSpo7Aodug0A9w],” a series revolving around a California murder trial, are available to stream at Crackle.com.

Our television critic Neil Genzlinger says[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/arts/television/8216sequestered8217-on-the-streaming-service-crackle.html] “you can watch it the way you read a beach novel: quickly, looking for the next plot rush, not taking anything very seriously.”

• Have you heard of Little Big Town?

They’re[https://itunes.apple.com/us/artist/little-big-town/id513770] the Country Music Association’s Vocal Group of the Year and the hosts of the three-hour special “C.M.A. Music Festival: Country’s Night to Rock[http://abc.go.com/shows/cma-music-festival].”

The show is a collection of highlights from a four-day party, with the genre’s hottest acts, that took place in Nashville in early June (8 p.m. Eastern, ABC).

• Inside the huddle.

Each football season, the “Hard Knocks[http://www.hbo.com/hard-knocks#/]” unscripted series follows a professional team through its five-week training camp. This year’s team is the Atlanta Falcons. Some believe that being on the show is a curse[http://grantland.com/features/the-questionable-perils-training-camp-microscope-offseason-stories-matter/] (10 p.m. Eastern, HBO).

• If you’re staying up…

On “The Daily Show”: Helen Thorpe, the author of “Soldier Girls: The Battles of Three Women at Home and at War[http://books.simonandschuster.com/Soldier-Girls/Helen-Thorpe/9781451668100]” (11 p.m. Eastern, Comedy Central).

“The Tonight Show”: Clive Owen, star of the new TV series “The Knick[http://www.imdb.com/title/tt2937900/?ref_=nm_flmg_act_3]”; Nina Dobrev of the CW’s show “The Vampire Diaries[http://www.cwtv.com/shows/the-vampire-diaries]”; the Head and the Heart[http://www.nbc.com/the-tonight-show/filters/guests/9361] perform (11:35 p.m. Eastern, NBC).

“Jimmy Kimmel Live”: Ted Danson; Eva Green of the forthcoming “Sin City: A Dame to Kill For[http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0458481/?ref_=nm_flmg_act_1]” and the show “Penny Dreadful[http://www.imdb.com/title/tt2628232/?ref_=nm_flmg_act_2]”; and a performance by Jake Owen[https://itunes.apple.com/us/artist/jake-owen/id128412737] (11:35 p.m. Eastern, ABC).

TOMORROW

• The Ebola crisis.

The World Health Organization[http://www.who.int.proxy.bc.edu/en/] convenes an emergency committee meeting in Switzerland.

• Weapon of mass destruction.

The Enola Gay, an American bomber, dropped an atomic bomb on the Japanese city of Hiroshima 69 years ago tomorrow. (A second was dropped on Nagasaki three days later.)

The bombing destroyed an area of about six square miles, killing as many as 80,000 people instantly and igniting fires that burned for days, killing many more. In all, 135,000 people died.

• United against unwanted phone calls.

About 10,000 computer whizzes will descend on the Rio Hotel & Casino in Las Vegas for the Defcon 22[https://www.defcon.org/html/defcon-22/dc-22-index.html] convention.

One anticipated competition, sponsored by the Federal Trade Commission, challenges hackers[http://www.ftc.gov/zapping-rachel] to build a device that lures in robocallers so it can figure out how to block them.

There is $17,000 in cash prizes, but more important, the chance to earn the gratitude of all humanity.

City Room
N.Y. / Region
New York Today: On Alert for Ebola
By ANNIE CORREAL
1063 words
5 August 2014
NYT Blogs
NYTB
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All rights reserved
Good morning on this hot Tuesday.

On Monday, a patient was treated at Mt. Sinai Hospital[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/health/patient-at-mount-sinai-has-ebola-like-symptoms-hospital-says.html] for a fever and gastrointestinal issues.

Because he had recently been to West Africa, where Ebola has claimed around 800 lives[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/01/world/africa/sierra-leone-declares-health-emergency-over-ebola.html?], the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention were called in.

The man was kept in isolation, his personal details guarded like state secrets.

In the evening, the city’s health department announced, “the patient is unlikely to have Ebola,”[http://abcnews.go.com/Health/york-city-hospital-treating-potential-ebola-patient/story?id=24838232] concluding that he was probably suffering from a more common condition.

The case highlights the difficulty of pinning down a disease that shares its most common symptoms[http://www.cdc.gov.proxy.bc.edu/vhf/ebola/symptoms/index.html] with everyday illnesses such as food poisoning.

It also shows that the city is on high alert.

Here’s where things stand:

• Quarantine officers[http://nypost.com/2014/07/30/airports-on-high-alert-for-fliers-with-ebola-symptoms/] are stationed at Kennedy and Newark airports.

• The health department has developed a plan to transfer patients arriving at Kennedy with Ebola-like symptoms to hospitals.

• All major hospitals in the city have at least one room designed to handle patients with Ebola or any other highly infectious disease.

• The city has told hospitals and doctors to consider Ebola when treating any ill patient who has traveled in Guinea, Liberia, Sierra Leone or Nigeria and to notify the health department.

• Adding to the collective concern, the city held its largest ever emergency drill[http://www.nyc.gov/html/doh/html/pr2014/pr025-14.shtml] on Friday, preparing 1,500 city workers for bioterrorism and outbreaks.

But the timing appeared to be coincidental.

The health department wrote on Twitter[https://twitter.com/nycHealthy/status/495304909042900993] last week that the exercise “was secretly in the works for months.”

Here’s what else you need to know.

WEATHER

Prepare to dab your brow. Sticky heat, with a high just shy of 90[http://forecast.weather.gov/MapClick.php?lat=40.78137333318327&lon=-73.96717071533203#.U-Ch3kh8FI7].

Thunderstorms are in store tomorrow.

And then a cold front from the Great Lakes should reel temperatures back in.

Ah, relief.

AND FINALLY …

There have been orange outlines of bodies[http://www.nydailynews.com/new-york/cyclists-ride-mark-spots-death-article-1.1484387], and white ghost bikes[http://nymag.com/news/features/47819/].

Now there is another artful — and unofficial — attempt to memorialize pedestrians and cyclists killed by automobiles.

It comes in the form of wings and roses stenciled on the sidewalk.

The Times[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/arts/design/memorializing-traffic-deaths-with-an-artists-touch.html?nl=nyregion&emc=edit_ur_20140805] followed the artist as she spray-painted her stencil around the city on Sunday.

Robyn Renee Hasty said she hoped it would cause people to look down, as if paying their respects.

The mother of a man killed by a bus watched as her son’s name was spray-painted below the stencil on 53rd street: “Seth Kahn, November 4, 2009.”

“Those wings are saying Seth is flying by, Seth lives here,” she said.

Kenneth Rosen contributed reporting.

Times Insider
Recommended Reading on Ebola
Erika Allen
206 words
5 August 2014
NYT Blogs
NYTB
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All rights reserved
Since spring, several countries in West Africa have been affected by the largest Ebola[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/subjects/e/ebola/index.html?module=Search&mabReward=relbias%3Ar%2C%7B%221%22%3A%22RI%3A5%22%7D] outbreak ever recorded. Denise Grady[https://twitter.com/nytDeniseGrady], a science reporter, has been covering the outbreak for The Times. We asked Denise to suggest reading for those interested in learning more about the disease.

Denise Grady:

The book “Spillover,” by David Quammen, has gotten very good reviews within the past year or so. “Virus Hunter: Thirty Years of Battling Hot Viruses Around the World,” by C.J. Peters, a famous infectious-disease expert, is also very good. It’s a bit older, but “The Hot Zone,” by Richard Preston, is also good. All three are quite compelling to read, and full of fascinatingly scary information that is presented in a really clear, understandable way.

I wrote a New York Times book for young readers, called “New York Times Deadly Invaders: Virus Outbreaks Around the World, From Marburn Fever to Avian Flu,” about scary, viral diseases, and it has a lot of information on Ebola and the closely related and very deadly virus called Marburg.

The Centers for Disease Control and Prevention[http://www.cdc.gov.proxy.bc.edu/vhf/ebola/index.html] and World Health Organization[http://www.who.int.proxy.bc.edu/csr/disease/ebola/en/] both have excellent information about Ebola on their websites.

Editorial
Opinion
Obama’s Africa Summit
By THE EDITORIAL BOARD
408 words
6 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Even as the Ebola virus serves as a reminder of Africa’s manifold challenges, a much brighter future for the Continent was on display in Washington this week, where more than 40 African heads of state are attending a summit meeting led by President Obama. Done right, with sufficient follow-through, the event should strengthen American ties to a continent that is expected to outpace China and India in population by 2040 and is widely viewed as the world’s last major economic frontier.

The event is a determined, and splashy, initiative by Mr. Obama to push back against other countries doing business there, especially China, which is investing heavily in infrastructure projects and using Africa as a source of vital oil and metals. It is also an opportunity to counter critics who say he has devoted insufficient attention to the continent.

Administration officials have been eager to persuade Africa that America’s democratic capitalistic system can offer advantages that China’s authoritarian system cannot. As Susan Rice, the national security adviser, said last week, “We don’t see Africa as a pipeline to extract vital resources, nor as a funnel for charity.” She described a broader vision in which the United States is committed to being a partner to create jobs, resolve conflicts and develop the human capital needed to build a better future.

To that end, the summit meeting was preceded by a gathering[http://www.whitehouse.gov/the-press-office/2014/07/28/remarks-president-town-hall-washington-fellowship-young-african-leaders] of 500 participants in Mr. Obama’s Young African Leaders Initiative. Other events included forums on women, health, trade, food security and civil society. Still others brought together American corporate executives with African leaders. The summit schedule will end with meetings with Mr. Obama on Wednesday.

The world has largely associated Africa with desperate struggles against war, poverty, famine and dictatorial leaders. But there are positive trends, too. Africa is home to six of the 10 fastest-growing economies in the world, an emerging middle class and markets that are increasingly open to foreign investment. In short, there is money to be made there.

There is also a growing threat from militants in some countries. And, as human rights groups point out, the summit meeting’s focus on trade, investment and counterterrorism cannot be allowed to dilute the Obama administration’s willingness to press for good and honest governance, fair elections and human rights, all essential for long-term stability and growth.

The New York Times Company

U.S.
Atlanta Hospital Admits Second American With Ebola
By ALAN BLINDER
948 words
6 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Correction Appended

ATLANTA — The North Carolina missionary who contracted the Ebola virus while working in Liberia arrived in Georgia on Tuesday and joined another aid worker in the specialized isolation ward of a hospital here.

Emory University Hospital admitted the missionary, Nancy Writebol, early Tuesday afternoon, after she was flown to Georgia aboard a private air ambulance that landed at a military base northwest of Atlanta. She arrived at the hospital in a motorcade that included an ambulance and an array of police vehicles. Onlookers crowded a nearby skywalk, and television news helicopters recorded footage that showed Ms. Writebol being pushed into the hospital on a stretcher.

Emory said that Ms. Writebol and Kent Brantly[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/03/us/kent-brantley-nancy-writebol-ebola-treatment-atlanta.html], a doctor who was working for the relief organization Samaritan’s Purse in West Africa, where Ebola has killed more than 800 people in recent months, were being treated in a unit built 12 years ago to isolate and care for patients with especially threatening infectious diseases.

“We are tremendously relieved that our mother is back in the U.S.,” one of Ms. Writebol’s sons, Jeremy Writebol, said in a statement. “We know that she will receive the best medical care possible at Emory University Hospital. We’re grateful to everyone who has joined us in praying for this moment.”

Bruce Johnson, the president of SIM USA, the Christian group with which Ms. Writebol is affiliated, said at a news conference that he had been told she was “very weak” but improving. He said that before the private jet departed Liberia’s capital, Monrovia, Ms. Writebol had eaten yogurt and walked, with assistance, to the plane.

“A week ago, we were thinking about making funeral arrangements for Nancy,” Ms. Writebol’s husband, David Writebol, said Tuesday, according to Mr. Johnson. “Now we have a real reason to be hopeful.”

Dr. Brantly’s wife, Amber Brantly, said Tuesday that she had visited her husband every day since he had arrived here on Saturday and that he “continues to improve.”

In a statement from Emory about Ms. Writebol’s admission, the hospital did not comment on the conditions of Dr. Brantly or Ms. Writebol, who are believed to be the first two people ever treated for Ebola at a hospital in the United States. Their admissions have drawn widespread scrutiny, and Emory doctors and public health officials have repeatedly countered speculation that Dr. Brantly and Ms. Writebol could easily infect others in the United States.

“Emory University Hospital physicians, nurses and staff are highly trained in the specific and unique protocols and procedures necessary to treat and care for this type of patient,” the hospital said in a statement on Tuesday. “The standard, rigorous infection-control procedures used at Emory protect the patient, Emory health care workers and the general public.”

Ebola is transmitted through contact with bodily fluids, and the people assisting Dr. Brantly and Ms. Writebol have worn suits intended to prevent contamination.

“We have a specially designed unit, which is highly contained,” Dr. Bruce S. Ribner, a specialist in infectious diseases at Emory, said last week. “We have highly trained personnel who know how to safely enter the room of a patient who requires this form of isolation. We have control over everything coming out of the unit, so that everything is made noninfectious before any materials come out.”

He described the protocols as “an inordinate amount of safety.”

Doctors elsewhere in the United States were on alert for the possibility of Ebola cases. At Mount Sinai Hospital in New York City, an ill man who had recently returned from West Africa was being kept in isolation[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/health/patient-at-mount-sinai-has-ebola-like-symptoms-hospital-says.html] while doctors investigated the cause of his fever and gastrointestinal symptoms.

The hospital said Tuesday that the man had been “stable overnight and in good spirits.” The Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, which is based here, was working to determine whether the man had contracted Ebola, but New York City officials were optimistic.

“Looking at his history, we think it’s highly unlikely that he has Ebola, but we’re going to await the tests from the C.D.C.,” Dr. Mary T. Bassett, the city’s health commissioner, said.

Dr. Ribner said last week that he was planning a regimen of “supportive care,” while waiting for the patients’ immune systems to fight off the virus. “We just have to keep the patient alive long enough in order for the body to control this infection,” he said.

The two received doses of an experimental serum before they left West Africa, but Mr. Johnson, whose organization has spent about $1 million for care and monitoring since Dr. Brantly and Ms. Writebol were found to have Ebola, said he was unsure whether Emory would continue to use that drug protocol.

Marc Santora contributed reporting from New York.

Correction: August 5, 2014, Tuesday

This article has been revised to reflect the following correction: An earlier version of a photo credit with this article named the wrong photo source. The picture of Nancy Writebol arriving at Emory University Hospital in Atlanta was by John Spink of the Atlanta Journal-Constitution, not of GAATJ.

World; Africa
Sierra Leone Deploys Troops in Ebola Crisis
By ADAM NOSSITER
584 words
6 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
FREETOWN, Sierra Leone — Faced with a widening crisis over the spread of the deadly Ebola[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/subjects/e/ebola/index.html?inline=nyt-classifier] virus, Sierra Leone[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/news/international/countriesandterritories/sierraleone/index.html?inline=nyt-geo]’s government said Tuesday that it would deploy hundreds of troops and police officers to ensure that patients and family members who may be infected remain isolated.

A battalion — 750 soldiers — will be sent to clinics housing Ebola patients in areas where the disease is most virulent, and the police presence will be reinforced at homes where family members are at risk of having contracted it, said Abdulai Baratay, a government spokesman.

Sierra Leone has become the center of the worst-ever outbreak of Ebola, which has instilled fear across West Africa and has spread to four countries. Sierra Leone, a country of 5.7 million people and the size of South Carolina, has recorded the highest number of cases, 646, and the second-highest deaths, 273.

The mortality rate is so high — up to 90 percent — that many across the region simply refuse to accept that Ebola is present. Several cases have been recorded here of families entering clinics to snatch away relatives with the disease and not abiding by new rules quarantining households — forbidding the coming and going of visitors — where there have been Ebola patients.

The deployment of soldiers and police officers announced Tuesday is intended to combat this wave, government spokesmen said.

It also appears to be a sign of the increasing anxiety of officials here, faced with a deadly tide that is intensifying, not diminishing. Monday was decreed a “stay at home day” in Sierra Leone, an interruption of normal life to bring home to a resistant population that it faces a deadly challenge.

“Where there is a serious situation, the president can invoke military assistance to civil power,” Unisa Sesay, the president’s director of communications, said in an interview. “You have to understand that there has been a lot of lawlessness connected to this Ebola business.”

Mr. Sesay noted that “family members have forced their way into these medical facilities and removed” patients “and taken them home, and overpowered the workers.”

He said the “infection rate” was increasing, precisely because of this lack of respect for the rigorous isolation measures that the disease demands. The troops will be deployed especially in the major outbreak centers of Kenema and Kailahun, in the country’s east, he said.

On the streets of the capital, people have made a joke of a “no handshake” rule counseled by some. But there were signs that the seriousness of the epidemic was taking hold. “Everyone is worried,” said Kingsley Sesay, 21, an automobile repairman in the Cline Town district. “Some are believers, and some do not believe. I’m worried, because it is a killer disease.”

The United States Agency for International Development announced Tuesday that it would deploy a team to West Africa, including some representatives from the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/organizations/c/centers_for_disease_control_and_prevention/index.html?inline=nyt-org], to help coordinate the American government’s response to the outbreak, which has so far killed 887.

World; Africa
African Leaders Sit Down With American Investors
By MARK LANDLER
1097 words
6 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON — President Obama[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/people/o/barack_obama/index.html?inline=nyt-per] convened a giant game of “Let’s Make a Deal” between the United States and Africa on Tuesday, bringing together nearly 50 African leaders with American investors for what he promised would be a long-term partnership that went beyond extracting “minerals from the ground for our growth.”

For Mr. Obama, the son of a Kenyan economist, it was the centerpiece of a three-day summit meeting of African leaders — some close allies of the United States, others barely on speaking terms — that is the president’s most ambitious attempt to cement his legacy as an American leader who cares about the African continent.

“Our entire trade with all of Africa is still only about equal to our trade with Brazil,” the president said. “I want Africans buying more American products; I want Americans buying more African products. I know you do, too, and that’s what you’re doing today.”

The African leaders seemed gratified by Mr. Obama’s sales pitch, which was echoed by a roster of boldface names from the business world, including the chief executives of General Electric, Coca-Cola and IBM and private-equity titans from Blackstone and the Carlyle Group.

But the chamber of commerce vibe was disrupted just before Mr. Obama spoke when some of the African leaders took umbrage to questions about the Ebola outbreak[http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2014/07/31/world/africa/ebola-virus-outbreak-qa.html] in West Africa and the security threat from Boko Haram[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/29/world/africa/boko-haram-targets-political-figures-in-string-of-attacks.html] and other Islamic militant groups.

“It’s an unfair vision of Africa,” said President Macky Sall of Senegal, referring to Western news reports about the outbreak. “Ebola[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/subjects/e/ebola/index.html?inline=nyt-classifier] is not an African disease,” he said, speaking in French. “It is necessary to confront Ebola as a threat to humanity.”

Tanzania’s president, Jakaya Mrisho Kikwete, noting that his country is in East Africa, said that too often “the whole of the African continent is perceived as if everywhere, everybody is suffering from Ebola.”

The presidents of Liberia and Sierra Leone canceled their visits to the summit meeting to deal with the outbreak. On Monday, American health officials met quietly at the State Department and with African officials from the affected countries to discuss their responses to the virus.

Mr. Kikwete also played down a question from Charlie Rose, the CBS News anchor, about the threat posed by militants, saying that Africa’s two major trouble spots, Somalia and Darfur, Sudan, had calmed down. That drew a sharp retort from President Moncef Marzouki of Tunisia, who cited continuing violence in Sudan and northern Mali.

“We have to face the problems, but we have to work together,” said Mr. Marzouki, who returned from exile in France to run for president after Tunisia’s autocratic leader, Zine el-Abidine Ben Ali[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/people/b/zine_elabidine_ben_ali/index.html?], was ousted in the opening days of the Arab Spring in 2011.

Mr. Obama steered clear of the Ebola outbreak or Islamic militancy — not to mention his own personal links to Africa. Instead, his speech focused heavily on creating the right conditions for American companies to invest in Africa. He made a couple of announcements to jump-start the effort, including $12 billion in new funding for the administration’s Power Africa initiative, which aims to provide electricity to households across sub-Saharan Africa. With $26 billion in funding, Mr. Obama said he had tripled the program’s goal of reaching 60 million households.

He also promoted $14 billion in new investments by American companies in Africa, including $5 billion from Coca-Cola. Heeding requests from African leaders, he said the White House would work with Congress to extend the African Growth and Opportunity Act[http://fas.org/sgp/crs/row/R43173.pdf], which grants trade preferences to sub-Saharan countries and expires next year.

With a narrative that has shifted from war and poverty to growth and development, Mr. Obama extolled Africa as an enticing opportunity for the United States. Though he did not mention China, he drew a clear distinction between the American approach and that of China, which has spent lavishly in Africa to lock up sources of minerals.

“We don’t look to Africa simply for its natural resources,” Mr. Obama said. “We recognize Africa for its greatest resource, which is its people and its talents and their potential.”

But if Mr. Obama sought to distance the United States from China, he delivered only a gentle admonition to the leaders about the need to curb corruption and install cleaner governments. “People should be able to start a business and ship their goods without having to pay a bribe or hiring someone’s cousin,” he said.

The conference was co-sponsored by the Commerce Department and the foundation of former Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg[http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/people/b/michael_r_bloomberg/index.html?inline=nyt-per] of New York, who told reporters: “It has become unfashionable to be a business person in the U.S. And Africa desperately wants to build a business class because that’s where the jobs are going to come from.”

While the mood at the Mandarin Oriental Hotel was buoyant, a few guests turned the tables on the United States. Mo Ibrahim, a Sudan-born cellphone tycoon, complained that American companies used legal techniques to dodge taxes in African countries where they have operations.

“Can you please also pay your taxes?” he said. “If you want to succeed long-term, you have to do clean business.”

With its combination of marquee panelists and ironclad security, the meeting felt like a cross between the World Economic Forum at Davos and a Group of 20 summit meeting. Corporate chieftains, and celebrity guests like former President Bill Clinton, mixed easily with African leaders, while reporters covering the conference were penned behind a rope and allowed to move around only under escort.

None of the African leaders will get one-on-one meetings with Mr. Obama, but the White House arranged for Vice President Joseph R. Biden Jr. to meet with the leaders of South Africa and Nigeria.

On Tuesday evening, however, Mr. Obama and his wife, Michelle, welcomed all the leaders to the South Lawn of the White House, where they were serenaded by Lionel Richie and served a dinner of grilled beef with coconut milk, and cappuccino fudge cake with Madagascar vanilla-scented papaya.

Andrew Siddons contributed reporting.

Your Wednesday Briefing
By ADEEL HASSAN and VICTORIA SHANNON
936 words
6 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Welcome to the Morning Briefing on NYT Now.

Here’s what you need to know for Wednesday:

• Trying to contain Ebola.

Experts at the World Health Organization began a two-day emergency meeting in Geneva today, where they will decide whether to call the deadly outbreak a global health emergency.

The move could involve imposing travel restrictions and border closures on affected countries in West Africa.

• Quiet skies over Gaza.

Hundreds of thousands of Palestinians are returning home[http://www.bbc.com/news/world-middle-east-28669031], many to find nothing left, on the second day of a three-day cease-fire.

A donor conference to raise funds for Gaza’s $6 billion reconstruction is being organized, and negotiations in Cairo[http://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2014/08/06/world/middleeast/ap-ml-israel-palestinians.html] on a long-term truce are expected this week.

• Gay marriage bans get scrutiny.

The United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit, based in Cincinnati, is hearing arguments today in six gay marriage cases[http://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2014/08/06/us/ap-us-gay-marriage-cases.html] from Ohio, Michigan, Kentucky and Tennessee.

Each case deals with whether statewide gay marriage bans violate the Constitution. Gay marriage is now legal in 19 states and in Washington, and there are legal challenges[http://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2014/08/06/us/ap-us-gay-marriage-state-lawsuits.html] to overturn all the remaining state bans.

• Executed.

A Missouri man was put to death[http://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2014/08/06/us/ap-us-missouri-execution.html] early this morning for raping and killing a college student in 1995.

He is the first prisoner killed since a lethal injection last month in which an inmate in Arizona took nearly two hours to die[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/07/25/us/a-prolonged-execution-in-arizona-leads-to-a-temporary-halt.html].

• You might want to reset your password.

A Russian crime ring has amassed[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/06/technology/russian-gang-said-to-amass-more-than-a-billion-stolen-internet-credentials.html] 1.2 billion user name and password combinations and more than 500 million email addresses, security researchers say.

If you’re worried, here’s what you can do[http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2014/08/05/technology/what-you-need-to-know-with-russian-hack.html].

• Focus on Africa.

On the third and final day of a U.S.-Africa Leaders Summit meeting, President Obama gathers with 50 African heads of state for discussions on investment, trade, development and counterterrorism, at the State Department.

Mrs. Obama and the former first lady Laura Bush host a symposium[http://www.whitehouse.gov/the-press-office/2014/08/04/first-lady-michelle-obama-and-former-first-lady-laura-bush-host-symposiu] with the African leaders’ spouses focused on public-private partnerships to pursue health, education and economic growth.

MARKETS

• Wall Street futures are flat to slightly higher. European shares are down moderately, and Asian indexes slipped.

• Two suitors give up: Rupert Murdoch’s 21st Century Fox[http://dealbook.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/murdoch-withdraws-bid-for-time-warner/] pulled the plug on its offer for Time Warner, and Sprint withdrew[http://dealbook.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/sprint-and-softbank-said-to-abandon-bid-for-t-mobile-us/] its bid for T-Mobile US.

• But Iliad, a French telecommunications upstart, is reportedly in talks to bolster[http://www.reuters.com/article/2014/08/05/us-tmobile-iliad-idUSKBN0G525A20140805] its $15 billion bid for 57 percent of T-Mobile US.

• Apple and Samsung , in another cease-fire, agreed to drop[http://bits.blogs.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/apple-and-samsung-drop-patent-fights-outside-the-united-states/] their patent lawsuits outside the United States. But the U.S. cases, their biggest, will continue.

• Walgreen’s, too, is waving a white flag, apparently giving up its plan[http://dealbook.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/walgreen-is-said-to-near-a-deal-to-buy-out-british-drugstore-chain-but-without-an-inversion/] to move its headquarters abroad to cut corporate taxes.

That came after Jacob J. Lew, the Treasury secretary, said his department was preparing options to wipe out the economic incentive[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/06/business/Action-in-washington-on-corporate-inversions.html] for such “inversions.”

Still, Walgreen’s appears to be close to a deal to take over the British pharmacy retailer Alliance Boots, even without a change in tax domicile.

• Both Fox and Time Warner release their latest earnings today.

NOTEWORTHY

• That’s about $45,500 a minute.

Three stars of the hit TV comedy “The Big Bang Theory” agreed to three-year contracts[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/06/business/media/big-bang-theory-stars-renew-their-contracts.html] worth $1 million per episode for each actor, putting them on the salary level of the actors on “Friends”[http://www.nytimes.com/2002/02/12/business/friends-deal-will-pay-each-of-its-6-stars-22-million.html] from last decade.

Production on the eighth season, which had been delayed, is to begin today.

• Shattering the glass backboard.

The N.B.A. champion San Antonio Spurshave hired[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/06/sports/basketball/spurs-hire-becky-hammon-as-nbas-first-full-time-female-coach.html] the league’s first female full-time coach.

Becky Hammon[http://www.wnba.com/playerfile/becky_hammon/], 37, will retire this month from the W.N.B.A. after a 16-season playing career before joining the Spurs as an assistant coach.

• Best and brightest.

The 15 global finalists in the Google Science Fair, an online competition for teenagers[https://www.googlesciencefair.com/en/], will be announced today. The grand prize, which includes a trip to the Galápagos Islands and a $50,000 scholarship, will be announced Sept. 22.

Entries include a device to diagnose cardiovascular diseases, a prototype of a wheelchair controlled by eye movements, and electricity-harvesting footwear.

BACK STORY

Corporate spinoffs are all the rage these days: Gannett just announced[http://dealbook.nytimes.com/2014/08/05/gannett-to-spin-off-its-print-business/] it would spin off its newspaper division, following similar moves by Tribune Co. and Time Inc., also in the media business, and many others across all industries.

How does dividing up a company into narrower, separately traded parts help?

In Gannett’s case, explains Michael de la Merced, a Times finance reporter, “spinning off newspapers means removing a slow-growing business, letting the company focus its time and money on better-performing assets like television stations.”

A benefit for company owners is that it’s a relatively tax-free transaction, as opposed to selling the division outright.

For investors, a spinoff gives them stock in two companies, letting them target the one they think has more potential.

While many spunoff companies tend to be slower-growing, Mr. de la Merced says, they also generate enough cash that they can pay handsome dividends.

For employees, the benefits aren’t as clear.

“The new companies have to be viable businesses, but the fact remains that they are smaller and have less of a financial cushion to fall back on,” he says.

Rick Rojas contributed reporting.

N.Y. / Region
Ebola Outbreak Hits Home for New York’s West African Immigrants
By WINNIE HU and NATE SCHWEBER
1034 words
7 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Gwendolyn Davis has never traveled to West Africa and does not know anyone who has contracted the deadly Ebola virus, but she is not taking any chances.

She makes sure not to hug or shake hands. She steers clear of coughing, and tries to avoid touching anything or anyone when riding the bus.

“There are people that come here from all over the world,” said Ms. Davis, 63, a retired teacher, as she folded clothes in a laundromat in the Bronx on Tuesday afternoon. “I don’t know what they’ve got.”

The Ebola virus has emerged as a new worry for some New Yorkers this week after a patient admitted to Mount Sinai Hospital on Monday was tested for Ebola, though doctors have emphasized that the probability that the test results will be positive is small. Some are worried for themselves; others are concerned about loved ones in West Africa, where a widening outbreak of the virus has already claimed the lives of more than 800 people. Two Americans who had been doing missionary work there are being treated at an Atlanta hospital. Ebola, which has no cure, is spread through contact with bodily fluids.

West African immigrants make up a small but growing part of New York City’s population. In 2012, there were 71,397 residents born in West African countries, including Ghana and Nigeria, up from 47,885 in 2000, according to an analysis of census data by the Queens College sociology department. About half of them — or 36,112 residents — live in the Bronx, but they are represented in every borough. There are 13,304 West African immigrants in Brooklyn; 10,142 in Queens; 7,636 in Manhattan; and 4,203 on Staten Island.

Many of these immigrants have closely followed the news coverage of Ebola, fearful that it has, or will, spread to the towns and cities they left behind.

“It’s like a shared panic,” said Kelvin Richards, the vice president of the Staten Island Liberian Community Association. He added that his mother, who runs a bread factory in Monrovia, Liberia, recently closed it rather than risk having a customer infect the workers.

“It’s like a ghost town in Monrovia,” he said. “Everybody’s freaking out, you can’t tell who has what because the symptoms are so general. It’s very crazy.”

In the Clifton neighborhood on Staten Island, which is known as Little Liberia, about 200 people gathered at a Lutheran church on Sunday to start raising money to help the Ebola victims, Mr. Richards said. Afterward, they set up a Facebook page, “Staten Island Against Ebola.” “We just came from a 14-year civil war, and now we have a virus killing people,” he said.

At outdoor tables in Clifton on Tuesday, women in floral print dresses recalled their anguish at not being able to reach family and friends back home, or their relief and joy when they did. “People are dying,” said Bettie Arkoi, 74, who spoke to several of her children and siblings by phone over the weekend. “Sometimes I sit down and cry, it’s so horrible. I’m asking God to protect them because I don’t think they are able to protect themselves.”

Rufus Quaye, 38, who has relatives in Grand Bassa, Liberia, stared at a news report on Ebola on a storefront television. “This affects us spiritually,” he said. “We have no idea the depths of how it’s affecting our families in Africa. It makes us go to God to ask for divine intervention.”

In the Bronx, the Islamic Cultural Center on East 166th Street has added Ebola victims to its prayers and plans to take up a collection to send to their families. The center serves hundreds of families from West Africa, many of them recent immigrants.

“I know a lot of people in these regions,” said Bakary Camara, a member of the center from Gambia. “There are some places where people don’t have enough to even eat and clothe their family. If they become infected, what happens to the family? They are destroyed.”

Mr. Camara, 49, a real estate agent, said the center had also urged its members to take precautions, if necessary, around people who had recently returned from West Africa. He added that their Islamic faith and teachings have helped them prepare for Ebola. For instance, he said, members always wash their hands and feet before they gather for prayer. They are also taught that if they live in a region with a plague, they should try not to go to another region unless they have an emergency, to avoid spreading it, he said.

“Nothing new surprises Islam,” he said. “We pray that this disease ends soon without taking many more victims.”

Outside the Royal A.M. African Market a few blocks away, Foulaba Jaloah, 49, recalled that when he was growing up in Sierra Leone and other West African countries in the 1960s and 1970s, the fear was catching malaria, which, he said, he contracted several times.

Mr. Jaloah, who has not returned to West Africa in years, said that he did not worry that he would contract Ebola in New York. But he said that others might. Already, he said, he has noticed a change when he meets people for the first time.

“I tell them, ‘I’m from West Africa,’ and they say, ‘Oh, Ebola,'  ” he recalled. “Everybody is afraid of everybody right now. When the scare comes, there is no friendship anymore.”

World; Africa
W.H.O. Weighing Emergency Decree on Ebola
By RICK GLADSTONE
724 words
7 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Scrambling to get ahead of the worst outbreak of the deadly Ebola virus, the World Health Organization announced Wednesday that it was considering the declaration of an international public health emergency and would convene a panel of experts in coming days to explore the use of experimental treatments for the incurable disease.

The announcements came as fears spread that a Saudi citizen may have brought the Ebola virus home to Saudi Arabia, which is still reeling from a mismanaged epidemic of Middle East Respiratory Syndrome that has killed nearly 300 people in the last two years. Saudi news media[http://www.arabnews.com/news/featured/612411] said that the citizen, a businessman in his 40s, died on Wednesday at King Fahd Hospital in Jidda after exhibiting Ebola-like symptoms, and that Saudi health officials had submitted biological samples from the patient to laboratories in the United States and Germany.

The Saudi accounts emphasized that the illness had not yet been identified, and they did not specify where the patient got sick. But Gregory Hartl, a spokesman for the World Health Organization in Geneva, said in an email that the patient appeared to have been in Sierra Leone on business in some of the affected areas.

The organization also announced[http://www.who.int.proxy.bc.edu/csr/don/2014_08_06_ebola/en/] 108 new Ebola cases recorded from Saturday to Monday, bringing the total to 1,711, with 932 deaths. Nearly all are in the three afflicted West African countries at the epicenter of the outbreak: Guinea, Liberia and Sierra Leone.

But in a sign of the spread of the disease internationally, the organization listed five new cases in Nigeria, Africa’s most populous country, bringing the Nigerian total to nine. The virus was discovered to have leapfrogged there by plane last week, carried by an American who had been working in Liberia and died in a Nigerian hospital.

The health organization said an emergency committee of international experts was evaluating whether the Ebola outbreak constituted a “Public Health Emergency of International Concern,” a classification that has only rarely been invoked to combat deadly contagions. The classification gives health authorities greater powers that include quarantining people in affected areas. The committee’s recommendations are expected by Friday.

In a separate announcement[http://www.who.int.proxy.bc.edu/mediacentre/news/statements/2014/ethical-review-ebola/en/], the organization said it was convening a panel of medical ethicists to explore the use of experimental treatments for Ebola, which has a mortality rate as high as 90 percent. The virus, which causes high fevers, aches and severe internal bleeding, is named after the Ebola River in the Democratic Republic of Congo, where it was first discovered in 1976.

“We are in an unusual situation in this outbreak,” Dr. Marie-Paule Kieny, the organization’s assistant director general, said in the announcement. “We have a disease with a high fatality rate without any proven treatment or vaccine.”

She said “we need to ask the medical ethicists to give us guidance on what the responsible thing to do is.”

Several experimental options are under development, including an untested drug that was administered to two American health workers from Samaritan’s Purse[http://www.samaritanspurse.org/article/samaritans-purse-doctor-serving-in-liberia-west-africa-tests-positive-for-ebola/], a Christian relief group based in Boone, N.C. They had been treating Ebola patients in Liberia and contracted the disease. Both were flown in recent days to Emory University Hospital in Atlanta and have been showing signs of improvement, causing an international debate over why Americans were given the drug when hundreds of Africans have died without access to it.

The World Health Organization’s announcement also cited the Samaritan’s Purse workers’ apparent response to the drug, saying it had “raised questions about whether medicine that has never been tested and shown to be safe in people should be used in the outbreak and, given the extremely limited amount of medicine available, if it is used, who should receive it.”

Denise Grady contributed reporting.

Business Day
An Obscure Biotech Firm Hurries Ebola Treatment
By ANDREW POLLACK
1607 words
7 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Inside special isolation units at an Atlanta hospital on Wednesday, Kent Brantly and Nancy Writebol, the two Americans infected with Ebola in West Africa, appeared to be responding to an experimental medicine devised by an obscure biotechnology company with ties to the Defense Department.

In West Africa, the estimated death toll from the outbreak kept rising, to 932, by the latest official count.

How and why Dr. Brantly and Ms. Writebol received the drug, ZMapp, is one of the many mysteries surrounding what has been called a “secret serum,” including the big one: Does it really work? The Americans are the only two patients who have been treated with the medicine, out of perhaps thousands who might have benefited so far, raising old questions about who does — and does not — have access to medications, including experimental drugs.

But the San Diego-based Mapp Biopharmaceutical[http://mappbio.com/], the primary developer of the drug, was consumed Wednesday with how to manufacture more of it with an eye to providing the drug to more patients, probably in the form of clinical trials.

“It’s absolutely overwhelming,” Larry Zeitlin, the president of Mapp, said in an interview Wednesday. “We are discussing with the F.D.A. the right path to make the drug available to people as quickly and safely as possible,” he added, referring to the Food and Drug Administration.

There were signs that efforts to increase production were beginning. Caliber Biotherapeutics, a Texas company established with funding from the Defense Department to respond to biological threats, has received inquiries from the government about whether it could help manufacture ZMapp, said R. Barry Holtz, the company’s chief science and technology officer.

The World Health Organization[http://www.who.int.proxy.bc.edu/en/] said it would convene a panel of medical ethicists early next week to explore the use of the experimental treatment in the outbreak in West Africa. Currently, neither ZMapp nor any other medicine or vaccine is approved for treatment of the virus, but there are several experimental options under development.

How quickly the drug could be made on a larger scale will depend to some extent on the tobacco company Reynolds American. It owns the facility in Owensboro, Ky., where the drug is made inside the leaves of tobacco plants. David Howard, a spokesman for Reynolds, said it would take several months to scale up.

Mapp was started in 2003 by Dr. Zeitlin and Kevin J. Whaley. They have worked together for many years, starting at Johns Hopkins University, on research that uses crops to produce immune system proteins to treat diseases in people.

Dr. Whaley, the chief executive of Mapp, and Dr. Zeitlin have not spoken much publicly since the news of the treatment broke on Monday, saying they are too busy and want to avoid the limelight. The privately held company has only nine people and has been financed solely by government grants and contracts, they said.

They say that major decisions on how to proceed are in the hands of public health authorities.

“We definitely would like to ramp up to have an impact on the Ebola epidemic,” Dr. Whaley said. But he added, “We’re not decision makers on many of these issues. There are regulatory and legal issues that have to be addressed.”

The drug had never before been tried in people, though it and some predecessor drugs had been tested in monkeys, showing some effectiveness. Mapp was only now gearing up to start the larger animal toxicity studies typically needed before testing it in humans, with an eye to doing the first human safety studies in healthy volunteers next year. For that reason there were very few doses available when the Ebola outbreak started.

Now the company is trying to move faster and it is likely the drug will be tested in patients rather than healthy volunteers.

ZMapp uses an approach called passive immunotherapy. Instead of having a vaccine stimulate the immune system to make antibodies that attack the virus, passive immunotherapy simply supplies the antibodies to the patients. For some infectious diseases, these antibodies are extracted from the blood of patients who have survived the infection and presumably have effective antibodies.

ZMapp instead consists of antibodies that are made by exposing mice to a key Ebola protein and harvesting their antibodies. Those antibodies are then genetically modified to make them more like human antibodies and therefore less likely to provoke an immune reaction if injected into people.

The gene for each antibody is then introduced into the leaves of tobacco plants using a system developed by Icon Genetics, a German company. The leaves then produce the antibody.

In one study published last year, three out of seven monkeys treated with antibodies developed by Mapp survived being infected with Ebola, even though treatment did not start until four days after the infection. Both animals in the control group died. The treatment worked even better in another study when begun earlier after exposure to the virus.

It turns out another tiny company, Defyrus, based in Toronto, was independently developing a similar treatment with the Public Health Agency of Canada.

The companies agreed to combine the best of their antibodies into a single cocktail called ZMapp, which is what the two American aid workers received. The data for ZMapp in monkeys has not been published but is said to be substantially better than for the predecessor drugs.

Dr. Anthony S. Fauci, director of the National Institute of Allergy and Infectious Diseases, said it was “too early to make any conclusions about how effective this intervention is,” since it was tested in only two people.

Still, there is interest in scaling it up.

Monoclonal antibodies produced by pharmaceutical companies for use in treating cancer or other diseases are typically made in genetically engineered cells of mammals grown in vats. But Mapp chose to use plants instead because large quantities can potentially be produced more quickly and cheaply.

About 15 years ago, it was thought that genetically engineered crops would be widely used to produce human proteins for use as drugs. When Dr. Whaley and Dr. Zeitlin were both researchers at Johns Hopkins University in 1998, they published a paper with Monsanto scientists about a soybean genetically engineered to produce an antibody against genital herpes. But that idea has largely been abandoned, especially when it comes to using food crops, because of concerns that drugs produced in crops might inadvertently get into someone’s cornflakes.

Even so, research has continued, funded by the Defense Department, on quickly producing proteins in tobacco plants that are grown inside buildings.

“You can start making protein within a matter of days,” said Robert L. Erwin, president of iBio, another company that is working on this technology.

Still, it seems that this product will take several months to produce in any quantity. The capacity of the Kentucky facility is small, meant to support only the small scale human safety tests that Mapp had been planning. The tobacco plants have to be grown for about a month before they can be infected with the antibody gene and the proteins have to be harvested and purified.

“Starting from zero and going to 60 is not so easy, but once you’re at sixty you can keep going at that rate,” Dr. Whaley said. He would not say how many doses could be made and how quickly.

Dr. Fauci of the infectious diseases institute said officials had been told it would be a moderate amount even after several months. “You’re not talking about thousands of doses or even hundreds of doses,” he said.

But there are some other companies that can also make drugs in tobacco, including Caliber, which has a larger capacity than the Kentucky facility. So the government is exploring using them to supplement production.

“We told them we have the capacity and are ready,” said Dr. Holtz of Caliber. He said that together Caliber and the Kentucky facility of Reynolds could produce large quantities in six months.

Dr. Michael V. Callahan, an infectious disease specialist at Massachusetts General Hospital who has responded on the ground to Ebola outbreaks, said the use of ZMapp for the two Americans raised some concerns given the limited existing supply. “What was the prioritization that allowed these patients to be treated?” he said.

According to the Department of Health and Human Services, the reason was that Samaritan’s Purse, the organization that employed one of the aid workers, contacted the Centers for Disease Control, which in turn referred them to National Institutes of Health, which in turn referred them to Mapp.

Doing clinical trials in Africa will likely require approvals from authorities there. And treating large numbers of patients with ZMapp, which needs to be given intravenously, could be difficult.

“What can you give in an austere treatment environment where there is very little nursing care?” asked Dr. Callahan. Authorities, along with the San Diego biotech company, are trying to figure it all out. Dr. Zeitlin said, “We’re kind of in uncharted territory here.”
