Op-Ed Columnist
Opinion
Fighting Ebola for Us All
By NICHOLAS KRISTOF
870 words
7 August 2014
NYTimes.com Feed
NYTFEED
English
Copyright 2014. The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
On July 23, Dr. Kent Brantly woke up with a fever. He immediately quarantined himself, and three days later a test confirmed his nightmare. He had the Ebola virus.

Brantly, 33, emailed a friend and said that he was “terrified,” for he knew better than anyone the horror of the virus. He had been treating patients in West Africa with it for many weeks, watching as they vomited, hemorrhaged internally and sometimes bled from multiple orifices — then weakened and died.

Some people have blamed Brantly and another American missionary infected, Nancy Writebol, for bringing the danger to themselves, even objecting to their return to Atlanta to be treated for the disease at Emory University Hospital[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/06/us/nancy-writebol-kent-brantly-ebola-atlanta.html]. For example, Donald Trump argued that Brantly and Writebol should not be brought back to the United States because of the risks involved.

“People that go to far away places to help out are great — but must suffer the consequences!” Trump said on Twitter[https://twitter.com/realDonaldTrump/status/495379061972410369].

On the contrary, this Ebola outbreak underscores why we have not only a humanitarian interest in addressing global health, but also a national interest in doing so. Brantly and Writebol are moral leaders in this effort and underscore the practical imperative of tackling global contagions early on. They deserve our gratitude and admiration because in Liberia they were protecting us as well as Liberians.

The human mind is very sensitive to threats from the likes of Al Qaeda. We are less attuned to public health threats, even those that claim more lives: Some 15,000 people with AIDS still die in the United States every year, according to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. It’s better to address a contagious disease at its source rather than allow it to spread.

“If we don’t fight to contain it there, we’re going to fight to contain it somewhere else,” notes Ken Isaacsof Samaritan’s Purse[http://www.samaritanspurse.org/article/samaritans-purse-doctor-serving-in-liberia-west-africa-tests-positive-for-ebola/], the Christian aid group for which Brantly works.

The World Bank has pledged $200 million[http://www.worldbank.org.proxy.bc.edu/en/news/press-release/2014/08/04/ebola-world-bank-group-mobilizes-emergency-funding-for-guinea-liberia-and-sierra-leone-to-fight-epidemic] to try to control the Ebola outbreak, but a tiny fraction of that sum might have contained it early on.

Dr. Thomas Frieden, the director of the C.D.C., cites an American-backed program in Uganda to train health workers to diagnose and contain Ebola. It worked. In 2011, a 12-year-old girl there caught the Ebola virus and died from it[http://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/18/9/11-1536_article] — but no one else was infected. It was an exceptionally rare Ebola episode that stopped after just a single case.

A similar program in West Africa might likewise have limited the human and financial cost of this outbreak, Frieden noted, adding: “An outbreak anywhere is a risk everywhere.”

This isn’t true only of the Ebola virus. Frieden recalls caring in New York for a patient from India with extensively drug-resistant tuberculosis, a complex case that cost $100,000 to cure. Later, a program was set up in the patient’s native village that could have resolved the case early for $10.

New York hospitals have been on alert for Ebola[http://bigstory.ap.org.proxy.bc.edu/article/new-york-hospital-patient-tests-negative-ebola], but diagnosis and segregation are complicated. I know because I was once such a suspected case.

Years ago, when I lived in Japan, I returned to Tokyo from Congo at the time of an Ebola outbreak there. One night a week later, I came down with a high fever[http://www.nytimes.com/1997/11/30/weekinreview/correspondence-sick-in-tokyo-i-lived-to-tell-about-japan-s-medical-miracle.html]. It felt like malaria, so I made inquiries about what hospital in Tokyo could best treat malaria the next day.

The health authorities heard “Congo” and “fever” and sent an ambulance staffed with people in what looked like spacesuits to rush me to a hospital. My neighbors were taken aback by the scene.

But, at the hospital, the emergency room night doctor knew nothing about tropical diseases. He poked me a bit, shrugged and told me to go home. (The next day, I confirmed that it was malaria.)

So don’t see Brantly and Writebol as reckless curiosities who somehow brought Ebola upon themselves. See them as leaders on the front line of an effort to help and protect Americans and Africans alike. We sometimes forget that health workers can brave significant risks — of infection with H.I.V., with tuberculosis, or even with the Ebola virus. Indeed, the staff treating Brantly and Writebol in Atlanta volunteered for that duty[http://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/03/us/kent-brantley-nancy-writebol-ebola-treatment-atlanta.html], and some offered to cancel vacation plans to help.

Bravo to them, and to so many health workers in Africa[http://www.nytimes.com/library/magazine/home/20010218mag-ebola.html] and America who try to halt the spread of disease — because it’s where humanitarian interests and national interests coincide.

“It’s natural to feel sorry for Kent” Brantly, a former medical school professor of his, Richard Gunderman, wrote in The Indianapolis Star[http://www.indystar.com/story/opinion/2014/08/01/fighting-survive-ebola-doctor-indy-answered-call-serve/13466427/]. “But I wonder if Kent wouldn’t turn this around. Instead, he might feel sorry for some of us, at least those of us shaking our heads in dismay at anyone who would travel halfway across the world to do what he did. A ship may be safest in harbor, but that is not what ships are for.”

I invite you to join me on Facebook and Google+, watch my YouTube videos and follow me on Twitter.

The New York Times Company

Ebola worries intrude on Africa summit; Obama vows partnership as continent’s leaders meet American investors
By MARK LANDLER
809 words
7 August 2014
International New York Times
INHT
8
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
The giant game of ‘‘Let’s Make a Deal’’ that President Obama convened here has brought together nearly 50 African leaders with American investors for what he promised would be a long-term and mutually beneficial partnership. ‘‘Africa,’’ the president said on Wednesday, ‘‘will help shape the world as never before.’’

But Mr. Obama also addressed a cloud that had been hanging over the conference, the Ebola outbreak that has hit West Africa hard. He said the United States and its partners would ‘‘continue to do whatever we can’’ to help, and ‘‘stand with the people of Guinea, Liberia and Sierra Leone.’’

The summit meeting, lasting three days, is the first in Washington dedicated to African leaders. And as the gathering neared its end, former President George W. Bush also made a rare return to the capital, on the sidelines of the gathering, to rally leaders behind a public-health campaign to conquer killer diseases in Africa and to forecast what he called ‘‘the beginning of the end of AIDS.’’

For Mr. Obama, the son of a Kenyan economist, the event represented his most ambitious attempt to cement his legacy as an American leader who cares about the African continent.

‘‘Our entire trade with all of Africa is still only about equal to our trade with Brazil,’’ the president said in a session on Tuesday. ‘‘I want Africans buying more American products; I want Americans buying more African products. I know you do, too.’’

The African leaders seemed gratified by Mr. Obama’s sales pitch, which was echoed by a roster of boldface names from the business world, including the chief executives of General Electric, Coca-Cola and IBM, and private-equity titans from Blackstone and the Carlyle Group.

But some of the African leaders took umbrage to questions about Ebola and the security threat from Boko Haram and other Islamist militant groups.

Tanzania’s president, Jakaya Mrisho Kikwete, noting that his country is in East Africa, said on Tuesday that too often ‘‘the whole of the African continent is perceived as if everywhere, everybody is suffering from Ebola.’’

The presidents of Liberia and Sierra Leone canceled their visits to the meeting to deal with the outbreak. On Monday, American health officials met quietly at the State Department and with African officials from the affected countries to discuss their responses to the virus.

Mr. Kikwete played down a question from Charlie Rose of CBS News about the threat posed by militants. That drew a sharp retort from President Moncef Marzouki of Tunisia, who cited continuing violence in Sudan and northern Mali, saying, ‘‘We have to face the problems, but we have to work together.’’

Mr. Obama, keeping his focus on investment, announced $12 billion in new funding for the administration’s Power Africa initiative, which aims to provide electricity to households across sub-Saharan Africa. Mr. Obama said he had tripled the program’s goal of reaching 60 million households.

Electric demands have been soaring amid strong economic growth in several countries. President John Dramani Mahama of Ghana, where economic growth has exceeded 7 percent, said in an interview that power demand there was growing by 10 percent a year.

With a narrative that has shifted from war and poverty to growth and development, Mr. Obama extolled Africa as an enticing opportunity. Though he did not mention China, he drew a clear distinction between the American approach and that of China, which has spent lavishly in Africa to lock up sources of minerals.

‘‘We don’t look to Africa simply for its natural resources,’’ Mr. Obama said. ‘‘We recognize Africa for its greatest resource, which is its people and its talents.’’

While the mood at the conference was buoyant, some guests turned the tables on the United States. Mo Ibrahim, a Sudan-born cellphone tycoon, complained that American companies used legal techniques to dodge taxes in Africa.

‘‘Can you please also pay your taxes?’’ he said. ‘‘If you want to succeed long-term, you have to do clean business.’’

Mr. Bush’s public-policy institute teamed up with Michelle Obama and the State Department to host a daylong forum Wednesday on education and health with the spouses of African leaders. Mr. Bush has made fighting AIDS in Africa one of his biggest priorities; his wife, Laura, also took part in the program at the Kennedy Center.

He said the battle against AIDS could be focused with better data, better treatment options and better prevention to ‘‘reach and help the highest-risk regions and groups.’’ He also urged African leaders to avoid discrimination that makes public-health efforts harder, although he did not specifically mention laws like the one overturned by a court in Uganda last week criminalizing homosexuality.

Sierra Leone to deploy military to enforce Ebola quarantine
By ADAM NOSSITER
435 words
7 August 2014
International New York Times
INHT
8
English
© 2014 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.
Faced with a widening crisis over the spread of the Ebola virus, Sierra Leone’s government has said it will deploy hundreds of troops and police officers to ensure that patients and family members who may be infected remain isolated.

A battalion, consisting of 750 soldiers, will be sent to clinics housing Ebola patients in areas where the disease is most virulent, and the police presence will be reinforced at homes where family members are at risk of having contracted it, Abdulai Baratay, a government spokesman, said on Tuesday.

Sierra Leone has become the center of the worst-ever outbreak of Ebola, which has instilled fear across West Africa and has spread to four countries. Sierra Leone, a country of 5.7 million, has recorded the highest number of cases, 646, and the second-highest deaths, 273.

The World Health Organization said Wednesday that the death toll in the four West African countries — Sierra Leone, Guinea, Liberia and Nigeria — had risen to at least 932, The Associated Press reported. Among the latest fatalities was an unidentified Nigerian nurse who treated an Ebola patient, the second death from the disease in Nigeria.

Also on Wednesday, the Spanish Defense Ministry said a medically equipped Airbus 310 was preparing to fly to Liberia to bring home a Spanish missionary priest, Miguel Pajares, who had tested positive for the virus and was being kept in isolation in a Monrovia hospital, The A.P. said.

The mortality rate from the disease is so high, up to 90 percent, that many across the region refuse to accept that Ebola is present.

Several cases have been recorded here of families entering clinics to remove relatives with the disease and not abiding by new rules quarantining households — forbidding the coming and going of visitors — where there have been Ebola patients. The deployment of soldiers and police officers is intended to combat such actions, government spokesmen said.

The military and police mission also appears to be a sign of the increasing anxiety of officials here, faced with a deadly tide that is intensifying, not diminishing. Monday was decreed a ‘‘stay at home day’’ in Sierra Leone, an interruption of normal life to bring home to a resistant population that it faces a deadly challenge.

‘‘Where there is a serious situation, the president can invoke military assistance to civil power,’’ Unisa Sesay, the president’s director of communications, said in an interview. ‘‘You have to understand that there has been a lot of lawlessness connected to this Ebola business.’’
